And sometimes administered in it are long alexanders, the roots of celestial lilies, and is lily: also wheat flour, and chicory. Use them plain or combined, grind them, and sift the powder: and powder it over the honey I mentioned, and use it.
If the case of the bladder extends and is not cured by this treatment: then you will know that corruption is deep within it: either it has made an impression on the bones or in the hard parts, or ligaments: then cure it by the treatment of fistulas, as was mentioned before.
Chapter on the care of ulcers and scratched nails, and incision of fingers, and amputation.
Ulcer under the nail of the thumb or big toe, and perhaps in other fingers is called panaritium.
When its duration is prolonged and neglected in treatment and cauterized with heat, it may become corrupted and discharge pus until it rots the nail root; and perhaps it corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foul smell; and the tip of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When you treat it as I have said, and your care does not do so: then it is necessary that you shave with iron the entire superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. Indeed, cautery is very helpful.
If the bone is healthy and the nail is also sound, and empty additions of the nail now press the flesh towards the inside and begin to pierce it, and wound it: then it is necessary that you put fine radium under the corner of the nail which pierces the flesh, and push it up; and cut the flesh easily, and put on top of it what remains of the flesh from burning medicines, corrosives, until it is removed completely. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then it is necessary that you cut out the bone, and remove it; for it will never heal as long as corrupted bone remains.
If you see the corruption and decomposition advancing towards the finger, then cut it at one node, according to its previous description. Then treat it until it heals.
If the nail happens to be rubbed or struck, and a strong pain occurs, then it is necessary that you use the leech first. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula crosswise from above to the desired length.
And be careful not to let the incision reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause a violent pain to occur on account of it, and it will become a cause for the growth of additional flesh at the site.
Then take care of the place so that it alleviates the pains.
The finger that is born in some human hands may perhaps be entirely fleshy; and perhaps some of them have bones, and perhaps there is a nail in it; and some of them are born at the joint, or its growth occurs in some knots of the finger. Therefore, the one that is born in some knots of the finger does not move; and the one that is born at the joint of the finger may perhaps move.
The dissection of the fleshy one (of the fingers) is therefore easy. This means to make an incision at its root with a broad spatula.
But the treatment of the one whose growth is at the root joint is difficult. Therefore, refrain from making an incision of it.
In the one that is born at the end of the knots of a finger, then it is necessary to make an incision in the first place through a round cut up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws that fit for this purpose. Then take care of it until it heals.
The consolidation that occurs in fingers often occurs either from the birth of a person (that is, it is born with him), or from a wound consolidation, or burns by fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut off that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place linen or gauze soaked in rose oil between them to prevent them from consolidating quickly, and separate them; or place a thin lead sheet between them until the site heals as it should.
And similarly, if consolidation happens to certain fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to how it is prepared and suitable for the shape of the member.
Chapter on Defects in Incision.
Uites are swollen veins filled with melancholic exudates that occur in many parts of the body; and most often they occur in the legs, especially those walking much and suffering, and those carrying heavy loads.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer a strong purging of the body from black bile at times.
Then perform venesection on the patient's basilica vein.
The cure of these (varicosities) with iron is according to two methods. One method is to make an incision and draw out black blood. The method is also that a vein is raised and drained completely.
The section [of the vein] however, is according to this method. Firstly, apply a poultice on the leg with much warm water until the thick and foul blood is resolved. Then tie the diseased leg from the upper part of its thigh to the lower part of its knee with bandage. Then cut the vein at one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then drain the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and it is necessary that a phlebotomist firstly attends to the diseased person. Then cut the nail downwards: until as much blood comes out as you see sufficient, and as much strength the diseased person can bear.
Then tie it and command him to abstain from foods causing black bile; and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and that does harm to the diseased person.
The method of draining is according to this method. Shave the diseased leg if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse it [the diseased part] in a bath; or apply poultice on the leg of it with warm water until it turns red and the veins swell up; or exercise it with strong movement if there is no bath present, until the limb becomes warm.
Then make a straight incision along the vein, along its length; or at the end of it near the knee, or at the lower part of it near the heel.
Then open the skin with knives and scrape off the vein from all sides, until it becomes visible; and at its appearance, it turns from red to black as if in a downhill slope. When it comes from the skin, it appears white like a sinew.
Then introduce the Radium under it until it is raised and comes out of the skin; suspend it with a blind hook, softly.
Then cut another section near that section with three fingers. Then scour the skin above the vein until it appears. Then raise it with Radii as you did before, and suspend it with another hook as you did at first.
Then cut another section or several sections as needed. Then raise it up and make an incision on the last section at the heel.
Then pull it out to the section above it.
And do this until you pull it out from the section above or higher sections, until it comes out completely; then make an incision on it.
If it does not obey you to pull and raise, then insert a needle with a strong thread and tie it and pull it out; or insert a Radium under it; turn your hand around all parts until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to raise it up and a harmful thing enters the patient from it.
When you pull it out completely, then put linen soaked in wine and rose oil or olive oil over the wound area and take care of it until it heals.
If the vein is twisted with a curved shape and has coiling in parts and not as we said in a straight manner, then you must cut across it in all its curved parts and the points of coiling itself.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it up completely; be careful at your cut not to cut the vein or wound, for it will be difficult for you to raise or pull it out. Preserve your prudence.
The form of incision to heal a wound.
Form of excision for cataract.
It is not like other incisions in sharpness nor has a pointed end to avoid damaging the vein; and it is roundish and flexible, not too fine because if it were too fine, it would cut the vein with its fineness; rather, there should be excess roundness as we have said.
Extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is generated in warm lands such as Alizegi and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands with little softness, that is, where no herb or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from putrefaction which occurs beneath the skin, just as it happens within the bodies of snakes and ascaris worms, and worms generated between skin and flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this vein is that which occurs from a heated inflammation in the leg. Then it is incised. Then the vein begins to emerge from that incision place, as if it were a root of a tree or an animal.
When that end appears, then it is necessary to wind around it a piece of lead weighing one keil dram, up to two drams and knots; and allow the lead to hang from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it towards the outside, wind that up in lead and knot it. If it extends too much, then cut a part of it, and wind up the rest. And do not cut it at the root before it has emerged entirely. Since if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh; it causes an abscess to occur, and pus in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated gently and drawn out gradually until it emerges from the end of it, and nothing remains in the body from it; and this vein emerges from certain people that is five palms long and ten. And now it has reached me, because the man has emerged from twenty palms.
If it is cut during your treatment time: then insert a probe into its hole and perforate it with a long rod until all that is in it from the matter is evacuated; and administer the putrefaction of the place with medicines for some days. Then treat it with treatment for boils (or abscesses).
And indeed this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot, or in the foot itself: and many openings occur to it, and a branch leaves from every opening.
Therefore treat it as we said in the section on ligature and with what was mentioned before.
Chapter on Surgery of Worms Generated Under the Skin, and it is called Boil Sickness.
This disease is called Boil Sickness in certain lands among us; because it often occurs in cattle and a small worm is born between the skin and flesh.
And it moves throughout the body, ascending and descending; and it is felt by the senses during its walking from limb to limb until it bursts wherever it bursts in the skin and comes out.
And its generation is from the decay of certain humors. As worms and snakes, that is earthworms and roundworms occur in the belly.
And it is feared from its malignity. Because when it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches a place, then perhaps it opens there and comes out, and destroys an eye; and this happens often.
When one wishes to treat it and extract it, then this is not done unless during its walking and appearance to the senses.
Therefore, tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it goes deep into the flesh, and you do not find it; then place a cautery on the spot with fire until it dies.
The greater fear is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If you find him (the wound) already advanced near the eye, then tie a good ligature below it (the wound). Then cut above it and extract him (the foreign body).
The sick person must use cleansing of his body with medicines that dissolve corrupt and bad humors.
And let him beware from foods causing corruption.
Chapter on the incision for Nakir's disease.
This disease is called Nakir in our land, and it is pain that occurs in certain members, then changes from member to member.
And indeed, I have seen it myself, as I am telling you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. And she pulled back her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm.
And after staying for an hour, I saw a swelling moving with the thread, like a worm, rapidly ascending to her shoulder as fast as possible; so that it was like live silver running from place to place.
The pain withdrew from that place, and it was fixed in the shoulder.
Then sat for an hour, and ran through the rest of the body, until it reached another arm.
Then he told me that he was turning over his whole body, as I had seen.
Therefore, I marveled at the speed of his exchange from one limb to another. And indeed before that, I had not seen this illness in my eyes in such a manner as I saw it now. Nevertheless, I have seen many who find pain to be transferred from one limb to another; and I myself did not see it as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it to be anything but because the woman was from those who are from dry villages with brittle veins; hence, it appears that the windiness was transferred.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this reasoning in those who are delicate and bodies with moist veins; hence, the concealed veins should not be considered.
Therefore, if you wish to heal him and the patient feels that pain; then, if it is visible to you as we have said, then press upon it quickly and beneath it; cut above it, until the windiness confined there is expelled; and cauterize the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your eyes: then heal him by pressing the body and that which expels windiness, and drive out the windiness with stinking pills and pills of serapion, and similar things.
Chapter on Extraction of Arrows.
The arrows are diversified according to their kinds and the places where they fall on the body.
The diversity of ears is as follows: because there are some with large points, and small ones; concave ones, and deaf ones. And there are those that have three angles. And from these are tongues. And from these are stars.
These, however, which are according to the limbs on which they fall, are according to two modes. Or the limbs are hollow from main limbs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidney, intestines, bladder, and similar ones. When it falls on any of these limbs and appears to you as signs of death which I will tell you later, then it is necessary that you draw out the arrow from them, for death follows him where it is, in many dispositions. And when these signs are not visible to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb, then draw it out yourself and take care of the wound.
The sign of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone and exits from the siphac (a membrane covering the brain) is: because such symptoms occur from it as sudden deafness, scotoma (diminished vision), vertigo, redness in the eyes, inflation, redness of the tongue, spasm, confusion of intellect, and vomiting of cholera. Blood may come out from the nose or ears. Speech may be cut off and voice destroyed. White moisture comes out from the wound place, like dough. And it comes out from there resembling a watery consistency of flesh.
If those signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of an arrow when it falls in the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and it feels as if it were fixed in a hard object, not in a soft one. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the pulse's movement; and blood runs black from the wound. And this is followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Therefore, know that death approaches without a doubt.
The sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels that follow the neck should be cut off, and the color of the patient changed. And it exhales a large, deep breath; and it seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow falls into the pleura, which is in the chest; then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great panting with violent pain, with sighing; and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
If the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested comes out from the wound.
If the arrow falls into the abdomen and becomes fixed there, something like feces or bile or a ruptured intestine comes out from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope for treatment or removal of the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out; and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger than the patient; then know that it is deadly.
The remaining limbs, as the face, neck, throat, spatula, assistant, vertebrae dorsales, pelvis, leg, and similar members are saved according to several arrangements; when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve; and the arrow is not venomous.
And I tell you what I have seen about these arrows, so that it may signify to you concerning your cure.
This is, because a man fell down with an arrow into his tear duct of the eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it from the other side, under his ear fat: and he was healed, and no damage happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell to him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not get anything more from it except its small end, which adhered to its wood: and the arrow was large among the arrows of the Turks, with a soft iron, in which there were no ears.
Therefore the Jew was healed, and no accidental evil happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian's throat.
And the arrow was Arabic, and it had ears. Therefore I cut above it between two cartilages: and already it was submerged in his throat. Therefore I sharpened myself with it until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who fell down with it into his abdomen: and we thought that he would die from it.
Afterwards, after thirty days had passed and nothing changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow: and I opened it up over it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no accidental evil happened to him.
And I saw a man who fell down with an arrow into his back, and the wound had consolidated on it. Afterwards, after seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
I saw a woman who had an arrow fall into her womb, and the wound healed upon her, and the arrow remained within her; and none of her conditions were changed, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural operations.
I saw another man to whom an arrow fell on his face: and the wound healed, and it remained, not causing him much pain; and similar things happened many times.
I removed an arrow from a prince of the King, which had fallen on his nose, and it had already deviated slightly towards the right; and the arrow was entirely hidden.
Therefore, I was called for his treatment after the fall of the arrow, up to three days. Therefore, I found the arrow wound very narrow.
I therefore asked her with a thin probe, and she did not feel it; and he himself was finding a puncture and pain under his right ear. Therefore, I hoped that this puncture was from the end of the arrow.
Therefore, I dressed the place with an attracting and digesting plaster, confident that the location would come out and a sign of the arrow would appear for me to cut over it; but it did not happen at the place where it was expected, indicating that the arrow had missed its mark.
I therefore continued with the plaster over him for many days; but it did not happen as expected, and the wound healed in the meantime; and the man remained weak from its extraction, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. I therefore applied sharp and corrosive medicine to that wound for many days until it was opened; and when I probed it myself, I felt a small end of the arrow adhering to wood.
Then I added the sharp medicine in the wound opening until it became clearly visible to us the end of the arrowhead; and it passed with that space of time approximately four months.
After the wound was opened and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, but it did not obey and came out; then I did not stop refining and devising ways on it until finally, on a certain day with properly made forceps according to the shape that comes at the end of the chapter, I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed the physicians judge that the nose cartilage cannot be restored. I therefore restored it, and the wound healed, and the illness became whole again; nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you about the quality of some arrow extractions; in order to put this rule and meaning on what I have not said.
Because the parts and distinctions of this art are not comprehended with the narrative; nor does the book contain it, and the wise artist compares a little about much; and with what is present, concerning what is absent: and he finds a new operation, and a new instrument for descents of foreign matters in this art when they occur.
Therefore I say that arrows do not come out of the limbs to which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by attraction from the place where it entered. Or conversely, by the other part.
And what comes out from where it entered is either a proceeding arrow in a meaty place; and it is attracted and comes out.
If it does not obey to come out at the hour when it falls; then it is necessary that you leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrefies; and the attraction of it will cease, and its coming out will take place.
And similarly, if it is fixed into the bone and does not obey you to come out, then leave it for a few days; and repeatedly it with attraction and motion every day until it comes out.
If it does not come out after a few days, then it is necessary that you bore around the arrowhead in the bone from all sides with a subtle drill until the arrows are expanded. Then draw it out and extract it.
If the arrow is already hidden in the body and concealed from sensation: then search for it with probes. If you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments that are suitable for drawing it out.
If you cannot apply pressure on it because of the narrowness of the wound and the length of the arrow in deep: and there is no bone, nerve or vein there; then mark it until the wound expands and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If there are ears he must hold, then freely remove the fixed flesh from them on all sides, with every skill you can, and skills, if it cannot be done by dissection of the flesh due to a fracture of the ears or their twisting, until it comes out.
When you perform an extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use twisting with your hand, along with tweezers in all directions, until you make it come out: and with subtlety of ultimate subtlety, so that the arrow does not break, and the attraction of it becomes difficult for you, and its extraction.
If you cannot do it by the hour, then leave it for some days until what is around it putrefies. Then repeat it: for the tunica will soften. If, however, the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what I have said in its treatment as mentioned in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or a nerve, or a tendon.
And administer your skill in every way possible for the extraction of the arrow: and let it be with facility, calmness, slowness, or firmness, as I told you.
It is necessary that, when you pull the arrow, it should be weakened according to its shape, according to which it was at the time of the arrow's accident. Because it is more beneficial. If that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible from the shapes.
The arrow that comes out from the opposite part or already proceeds to the outside, or makes a hump at the end of the arrow by the sensation of the skin on the upper side and you see it bulging: cut above it, and the cut should be according to the amount that the tweezers can grasp.
Then pull it out: for it is easy to come out by itself.
If it remains in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness until the arrowhead impresses into the bone and expands; then pull it out.
And if not: let it be for a few days; then draw it again until it comes out.
If the arrow shaft is wooden in it, then force it out with it.
If the arrow shaft fell out, and force is to be administered: then insert a concave instrument into it so that its concavity enters the shaft's tip; then force it with it.
If the arrow is concave, then force it out with the inserting instrument. For the arrow is loosened by that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut out all the flesh where the poison is: if it is possible for you to do so. Then care for it with what is suitable for that purpose.
If the arrow falls into the chest, or the abdomen, or the bladder, or the side: and is near where you can reach it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut over it: then cut over it, and avoid cutting the vein, or nerve, and remove it.
Then sew up the wound, if you see one, and take care of it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
The tips should resemble a bird's beak, which have been made like iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not let it go.
And they are made in various shapes, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, and the width and depth of the wound.
Form of the Probing Hook concave.
And the form of the Probing Hook for the deaf.
This is the tip of the probe similar to a radius, so that it can be relieved into the concave arrowhead and pushed along with it.
Chapter on Phlebotomy of Veins.
Veins for bloodletting, the custom of which runs in the body, are thirty.
From the head there are sixteen: two pulsating veins which are after the ears, called the two Herbs: and two arteries which appear in the two temples: and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Watchers: and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead: and a vein at the end of the nose. And two jugular veins, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins that are to be bled in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the side of the woods; and they call it by the common people the vein of the head. And Black. And the Middle one, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalic; and they call it by the common people the vein of the body. And Basilica, and it is placed on the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And they call it by the common people the vein of the abdomen. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed on the elbow; and that is the one which is bled, and that is the one which appears above the thumb with a clear presentation.
And Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the wrist and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. The one of them is which is a curvature under the nail of the thumb from the side of wood. And the second one (is) Saphena: and its place is at the heel, on the domestic side. And vein Nessus: and its place is at the heel, on the wilderness side.
And in the other limb (there are) three veins similar to those (mentioned before).
[Title: On bloodletting of two veins after the ears.]
The bloodletting of the two veins that are after the ears is beneficial for ancient catarrhs, and for hemigraine (headache), and for scrofula (tuberculous swelling of the lymph nodes in the neck), and for old maladies of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting (it) is according to what I have said (in previous text). And (it is) to shave the head of the sick person: and his back of the head should be rubbed in the place of two veins with a rough cloth rubbing well. Then the sick person should bind his neck with ointment until two veins appear. And their places (are) after the ears, in two depressed places of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark it with a hot iron (branding).
Then take the bloodletter (phlebotomist) with a cutting instrument (cultellus), and that one is called 'The One Who Cuts'. Then insert it (the cutting instrument) into the vein under the skin, until the bloodletter reaches the bone. Then draw your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut (make a shallow incision) in the vein along with the skin. And let the length of the incision be the size of two fingers joined, or about that: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then bind it with sutures, and let it heal by itself.
And now begins the first part of the book: incision and cauterization [of the affected area].
Venesection of the two arteries in the temples is beneficial for an old headache called hemicrania; and it helps for sodium praua, obtalmia assidua, and excess discharge of sharp fluids to the eyes.
And the method of venesection should be as I have told you. The sick person should bind his neck tightly until two veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulse is felt under your finger. Then you will mark them with a heated iron.
Then scrape the skin above the vein with your index finger and introduce the lancet under it, and scrape the vein towards the top. And cut it as you did with the two other veins, and let it bleed according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction of the sick person, and place your finger on the vein for an hour. Afterwards place a cloth over it and sutures, and bind it tightly; then let it heal by itself.
And now ends the reminder of these [procedures], and their incision and extraction, at the beginning of the book.
The benefit of venesection of the forehead vein is after venesection of the temporal veins, for old facial ailments; and for headache, redness, and foul ulcers.
The quality of phlebotomy [is] as I have told you. Compress the sick neck with a poultice until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called Fossorium.
Place the sharp point that is at the top of the Fossory over the same vein, and strike it with a chisel or with something else in the same manner; and allow blood to flow according to the amount you desire. Then solve the wound of the sick person, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad leech. However, it should not be the sharp-edged leech, as the other leeches, but rather the broad-edged one. And the leech should be applied across (the wound) because the bone is nearby; and perhaps it will break when it is sharp.
The remedy for the two veins that are in the two lacrimal ducts of the two eyes is for the eye affected by scabs and redness; it is applied to the eye itself, and to the diseases of the face.
And the quality of the leeching of these veins is that the sick person should bind his neck with a bandage. Then apply the leech to them; and you be standing over his head.
And make the leeching across (the wound) to a length less than the width of the leech with a small leech and a broad one slightly smaller; because there is no flesh in that place. If the leech is sharp-edged, it may break.
Then let blood flow from the wound as much as is necessary for you. Place a piece of cloth over them and bind them overnight; and then unbind them.
The veins of the nose are useful for treating acute fever, violent sore throat, diseases of the face such as headache and redness which occurs on the face; especially when it is chronic.
The quality of phlebotomy in this case is to tighten the sick man's neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand and take a fine, long phlebotomy needle; and fix it in the middle of his leper's snout, between that which distinguishes the nose according to its correct position. Since the vein is not visible there and because blood begins to flow immediately.
It is necessary that you hold your finger with the phlebotomy needle a little: and make the blood run according to your need. Then tie it up at night. For it heals quickly.
Guidegi's two methods of phlebotomy are beneficial for the constriction of coughing, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and impetigo, as well as bad ulcers, and those that corrode, namely herpetic sores or scabies.
The quality of the phlebotomy is to tighten a sick person under both necks with a band; and the worker stands above the head of the sick man, and the sick man sits on a chair.
Then the vein is to be phlebotomized at a length slightly larger than the width of the phlebotomy needle. Then draw out an amount moderated or according to what you see, due to necessity for that purpose. Do the same with another vein.
Then untie the band and tighten two veins with a constrictor; so that the sick man does not choke. And release it until morning. A wound heals.
Chapter on the Phlebotomy of Veins (Algeberich). Algeberich's veins are beneficial for phlebotomy because they contribute to alcohol in the mouth, and decay of gums, as well as bad ulcers, fissures of the lips, ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
The quality of phlebotomy is that you make the sick person sit before you: and tie his neck with a bandage. Then roll back his lip, and look at the two veins: one is on the right side of the lip, and the second is on the left: and the demonstration of each is by the blackening of both. And that is true: because around them are small, dark veins. Therefore make an incision on each with a cutting incision.
But if there is doubt about you and you do not know which one it is: then direct yourself to the larger incision, and that will be more obvious.
And similarly, do the same with the two veins that are on the upper lip: and certainly there is a common custom with it: and make an incision of the two veins that are on the lower lip.
[Paragraph on Phlebotomy of Sublingual Vein After Cephalic Phlebotomy.] But of the two veins that are under the tongue, the help for phlebotomy is: because phlebotomy of the cephalic is for swellings that are in the throat, and sicknesses of the windpipe, and sicknesses of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the sick person sits between your hands facing the sun: and clean his tongue, and look below the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein: and the color of each declines towards blackening.
Therefore phlebotomize both: and be careful not to injure your hand while phlebotomizing them. For there are arteries beneath it, and perhaps there will be a flow of blood from these arteries.
The three veins that are phlebotomized in the elbow, are in whose phlebotomy a common custom runs in all men.
And phlebotomy of these (things) is done according to two modes. It can be done either by pricking with a broad-bladed leech (myrton): or by applying oil to the puncture for thinning: or by cutting with a sharp-edged lancet (cultellus), and this is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And among these (things) there are broad and thin forms according to the width and narrowness of the veins. The form of the leech (myrton) should be broad, as you see. It is suitable for opening hollow veins that are full, projecting, apparent, thick and those that contain thick, cloudy blood.
And this leech (phlebotomus) is smaller in width and more delicate for thinning (extirpation).
It is suitable for the phlebotomy of fine veins that contain subtle, yellow blood.
The basilica vein (one of the three) is a help for phlebotomy (bloodletting). Because it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, because it follows the chest and abdomen; it is suitable for the leech (phlebotomator) when he performs phlebotomy on it, to be careful, and to beware of it since below it is an artery. If (the leech) therefore errs and adds in the puncture of the leech, he will cut the artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore, the leech (phlebotomator) should not prick it with a leech: rather he should cut it with a lancet (Alnesil).
If the basilica is not apparent with a clear manifestation, then it should be sought out and exchanged for another or a branch of it should be sought: or if no branch is found, the leech (phlebotomus) can take the arm in its place. The matter is evident, and cut it with the leech Anesil, as we said.
If you wish to perform phlebotomy on the same, then it is necessary before tightening the arm, so that you touch the place, until you know the place of pulsation. Then mark the place with pitch, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave knife by the leech Alnesil, as we said. And beware that the blow does not fall into the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, while tightening the ligament, swelling at the place which you marked with pitch, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery; therefore avoid it.
If you see blood springing up at the phlebotomy, like a boy's urine, and it is subtle, red: then you know that it is from the blood of the artery. Therefore approach, and place your finger over the same space, afterwards remove your finger. If therefore blood is cut (many times it is cut), then tighten the arm and let it go; and beware the weak from moving it; let there be fear of it.
And do not move it for some days, until it heals.
If however blood is not cut, and it beats you; and if medicine is not present to you at the moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its ends contract, and blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a fig tree, and fold it; take half of it, and tighten it over the place of vein tying, with a ligament and wool until another day.
Then if blood is cut off, that is good. And if not [the case]: then care for the self with him to whom it preceded, the remembrance of the place from which powder-producing drainage flow is abscinding; and the cutting off of his blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the smallness of the wound's location, and the possibility of ligament from the arm.
However, the bleeding of the dark vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; for this reason, since it is composed of a branch of Basilica and a branch of Cephalica, as we have said.
Furthermore, it is suitable for the bloodletter to be cautious about it, since there is a nerve beneath it. Therefore, if the bloodletter adds too much during the bleeding and finds a nerve, there results numbness, and healing becomes difficult, and perhaps he will not recover completely.
And this nerve often appears to the sense of touch. If it is concealed in some people and is subtle and does not show itself, then you must place your bleeding where you bleed her, with the incision of Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with all your might.
If the vein is between two nerves, then pierce the vein in length.
The Treatise on the Vein of the Head. But the bleeding of the vein of the head is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and contributes to eye ailments.
And it is suitable for this vein in particular, if you wish, that the bloodletter should anoint it with oil or myrtle while bleeding, since it is safer than all veins, because it is not beneath an artery, nor a nerve.
It is appropriate for you in bloodletting that you ask the barber to take only the head of an arm, and seek a softer spot; it does not harm him if no such place is found at first strike, that you should repeat on it with bloodletting several times.
Perhaps an abscess occurs in some people when they are not bled at the first strike, but it does not harm him that abscess.
Chapter on the usefulness of bloodletting and precautions to be observed. The nature and circumstances of bleeding, and those matters about which you should think for correction.
Firstly, know that bleeding is not administered unless for the preservation of health and persistence thereof, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And bleeding should be done in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the radial (fusca), and the basilic. And bleeding should be done at the beginning of spring after fasting, during summer and autumn, and winter after eating when signs of filling appear.
Bleeding should be done on the first or third day, after three hours have passed since the previous day.
However, bleeding administered in illnesses has no determined hour; but whenever it is necessary and appropriate for that purpose, it may be done at night or day, and in any hour, and at any time.
It is not necessary to bleed children until they reach the age of fourteen. A pregnant woman should not be bled from the fourth month up to the seventh. The elderly who have passed sixty years should not be bled.
When someone wants to be bled for any reason, it is necessary that they clean their intestines with a mild enema before the bleeding if there is much retained feces in them; so that dirty, harmful fluids from the intestines do not attract veins at the time of bloodletting and harm the main organs.
The patient should not be bled when he is in a state of satiety or nausea: neither the drunkard, nor one coming out of drunkenness, until that (condition) has been removed from them. The sick man should not be with dropsy.
Care should be taken not to bleed again after a colic attack, vomiting, diarrhea, increase in sexual activity, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and any other thing that weakens the strength of the body or the animal.
Then consider in detail about the fluids before that (bleeding), if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medications; if possible for him.
Then enter a bath, if it does not forbid him something; or exercise in some way to make the blood thinner.
And put the bleeding of his in the chest, around the third day, as we said; and strive to make him expel his chest on that day from all animal ailments: such as suffocation, anger, fear; and from all bodily ailments, such as labor, punishment, excess, and similar things.
And let him sit with the things that are in use by human custom for their aroma, scent, fragrance and similar things in quantity according to his ability.
Then let the bleeder sit on a higher place than the bleeding couch; afterwards he shall draw his arm out and rub it with his own hand twice or thrice. Then tighten the bandage with a cord and wrap it twice.
And let the bandage be moderate, because if the bandage is not equal or too tight in the binding, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the ligature (stricture), it is necessary that the phlebotomist rubs his hands together simultaneously, until the veins swell, and they become apparent to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist cleanses (the instrument) for phlebotomy with a little oil, in an ancient and proper way.
Then he places his index finger of the left hand on the same vein at the place where he wishes to do phlebotomy, barely: so that the vein does not escape and the blow is cushioned (absorbed). Since the veins are like strings fleeing during phlebotomy and from them is that which is full of windiness; and when you place the phlebotomizer upon it, it contracts under the phlebotomizer, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein: and if he opens it, it is ligated; therefore for this reason it is necessary that the phlebotomizer be firm and attentive in all these matters.
Then he places down (the instrument) the phlebotomizer. If then a vein is opened from one side, it is good. And if not, then do it again on the other side in the same place barely or above it with haste; if the spot is not pierced through (repeatedly).
If it is pierced through or frightened by the weak, then dismiss him for a day or two; and do not tighten the ligature. Perhaps (the wound) will attract an abscess that is hot. Do not enter the bath.
Then repeat the phlebotomy, if it is loved (desired).
If the opening is small or the blood flows thinly; and be careful that the amount of blood which you wish does not escape from the blood; then repeat the phlebotomy in the same hole according to correctness, and add slightly in the operation.
Make it quickly before the location is removed, for the location to be removed is in many people during a small operation.
If you see it already removed, then do not go over it at all, for nothing exempts you from it in your presence. And place something made of oil over it: because it soothes the flow of blood. It is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
Similarly administer the ointment of oil in every phlebotomy of yours, where you let blood against the flow of blood: and they do it with Tyrian alfarach, and sageza, when one of them is placed over the place.
Blood becomes subtle and dissolves when it is thick.
If it happens in the place of phlebotomy a large abscess, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or it is a small opening of a vein, then hurry and place over the place a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated slightly: and tie it for an hour: because it dissolves.
It is necessary that you do this after the blood has come out from the vein itself, when it has completed its flow, or from another vein.
If something remains in the place after several days that is black or greenish, it does not harm it: and if you wish, place over it something of aloe, or myrrh dissolved; or something from the juice of mint, that is, calaminthe or similar to it.
And indeed abscesses and protrusions often occur in the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore place your hand over it, then if you find softness under your pressure on it, then that protrusion is bad. Carefully place something from what I said upon it. Perhaps blood from the artery will flow out of it.
Make an ointment for it with that which has styptics, so that it may harden the place. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.

If the blood is black, then let it come out until it turns red; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it come out until it becomes more subtle; and similarly if it is sharp, let it be removed until its sharpness disappears.
It is necessary for the one who is full and strong, and needs to have blood drawn once, that the vein cutting be amplified; and let him be a vein cutter with one side; and it should be opposite for the one who is weak.
It is necessary to draw it out in veins and that the hole be tight; and it is better when administered in vein cutting that it be a cross section, not a prick.
This method of vein cutting is safe from the flow of blood and from the cut of a nerve; it is more praiseworthy and safer than vein cutting by width and length.
And whoever is accustomed to come for vein cutting with fainting, it is necessary that he eat a little bread soaked in water of grape-juice or sekanjabin if he is heated; and draw blood from him in three or four veins.

But if one wants to empty his arm and let out blood, and his body is probably: then he should do this after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy.
If one wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it inclines, then it should be done on the second or third day.
In a body where much blood has already become heated and sharpened, and it makes fever occur: it is necessary for the blood to be expelled from him once, and a large quantity to be drawn out; and the opening should be enlarged until syncope occurs; after you have observed all conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on the pulse of his arm along the course of the blood, so as not for death to occur due to syncope. For it often occurs when the phlebotomist is unaware, and it falls through negligence.

But if it is during the days of summer, then you are excused from dipping your hand in hot water. It is indeed recommended that the course of blood be let in hot water during winter time.
This vein's bleeding is safer than all other veins because there is no pulsating vein or nerve under it.
Phlebotomy of the right hand (Salvatella) is for the afflictions of the liver.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that you tie the little finger of the hand with a ligature or your hand after inserting it into warm water until the vein becomes inflamed and it is clearly felt. Then make an incision across (it). And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And preserve (it), lest you cut off your hand with the phlebotomy instrument. For under it are the nerves of the fingers exposed from the flesh.
Then put your hand again into warm water and let blood flow there. For if you do not repeat it in warm water, the blood will clot in the vein opening and stop flowing.
Therefore when you extract from the blood a quantity necessary, then put oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And likewise it is necessary that you do this with every constricted vein.
But the aid of this phlebotomy is from the left hand, because it is for the afflictions of the spleen; and do likewise with its phlebotomy as you did with the second equally.
And the aid of the saphenous vein phlebotomy is for the afflictions that are in the lower part of the body, such as the afflictions of the womb, retention of menstruation, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs and similar afflictions.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that an afflicted person puts his foot into warm water; and he should do rubbing on it until the veins swell up, and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. The location of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb; and many branches originate from it on the sole of the foot.
The vein therefore should be tapped in a larger branch of it, or at the heel in its cluster; because it is better and safer.
If the vein is to be tapped on the sole of the foot, then take care not to touch the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
Place your lancet for tapping the vein at an angle across it, as if you wish to cut into it; and let the one who taps the vein be called Nesil.
If the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse his foot in warm water; and let the blood run out into it until it has emptied.
If the vein is accidentally missed by the one who taps in the first instance, then let him repeat with the lancet towards the upper part, for it is a safer place, and there is no danger of harm, provided you take care to avoid the nerves, as we have said.
Similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot.
The location of the Sciatic vein, as we have said, is near the heel on the side of the woods.
The aid for tapping this vein is to relieve pain in the ankle when it is due to hot blood.
The quality of the tapping is that an unwell person should enter the bath and hurry; and tie his leg from the side of the ankle up to the heel with a long, fine bandage; for it will not appear unless for this reason.

[Section on] Venesection, and the quality of its use.

And venesection is done according to two modes. With scarification and the drawing of blood; and the other is venesection without scarification.
And this [form of] venesection that is without scarification, is according to two modes. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the places for venesection with scarification and drawing of blood on the body are fourteen. One of them is the venesection of Alnocrates, and it is at the end of the head; and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; and the venesections of Alhagdahim, and they are two blades of the neck from both sides simultaneously; and the venesection of the brain, and it is under the lower jaw; and the venesections of two spoons; and the venesection of Alachosos above the end of the tail; and the venesections of two kidneys, and they are in the middle of the arms; and the venesections of the legs; and the venesections of the ankles.
And venesection does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of this its power is not drawn out from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. It is not necessary to administer ventosation (ventosection) with or without fire, until the entire body has evacuated, in any ailment caused by retention.
If necessity requires ventosation due to an ailment or custom, we administer it at the beginning, end, and middle of each month, as well as at any time it may be required.
That is, because from some people there arises an excess of blood, until it requires extraction through ventosation, a pain and headache are found in the head.
And among them is one who finds retention and redness on his face, head, and neck. And among them is one who finds itchiness on his face and forehead, as well as darkness and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the site of his ventosation. And among them is one whose laughter multiplies. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or the tongue protrudes and spits out the blood. And among them is one whose sleep multiplies. And among them is one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, as well as the dead, wounds, and similar things.
When we therefore see something from among these, especially if it is in the middle of the third month: we then prescribe ventosation to be done, after two or three hours have passed since that day.
The benefit of ventosation for the neck is that it relieves pressure in the head and that which is drained goes to the eyes. However, it must be done after the evacuation of the entire body.
And this ventosation is in place of cephalic phlebotomy (venesection).
It is not allowed to use it who is of a cold temperament, or where there is catarrh. It causes him great harm.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for the elderly to use it, and where there are cold diseases of the head.
He who becomes constant over it generates forgetfulness in him.
Therefore, you must instruct the ventilator to lower his hand with the wind for a short while towards the bottom, fearing the production of forgetfulness.
Ventilation or Alkehel is in place of phlebotomy for Fuscus, and phlebotomy for Basilica, and therefore it produces coughing, constriction of breath, shortness of breath due to the instrument, coughing, and filling (of the body).
It must be forced to ventilate Alkehel briefly, because if it is placed below, it generates weakness in the heart and the stomach.

Therefore, you must instruct the ventilator not to press his hand during scarification, so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
But ventilation under the chin causes alcohol in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar conditions which are in the mouth, and takes the place of phlebotomy elsewhere which are in the two lips.
But ventilation with spatulas causes a condition called alchafacham, which comes from compression and heat.
But ventilation of the internal parts of the two fists causes help for phlebotomy of the veins of three (types), Basilica, Fuscus and Cephalicus: because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh; and these small veins draw blood from other larger ones until the attraction reaches the three veins.
It is necessary that you advise the Scarifier not to proceed too much in scarifying: because the place is bare of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The one who is ventosed on the anus, relieves hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
It is necessary that you advise the one who performs ventosing, that the ventose should be large and made of air: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps delicate ventoses may break; and it shall be scarified by a deep scarification.
Ventoses of the legs decrease with a clear reduction: because they draw blood from the whole body, and bring about old pains in the kidneys, and uterus, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and bring forth water retention and abdominal dropsy: and they stand where phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
Nevertheless, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
The help of the ventose used in alhorcobeni is close to the help of the ventoses of the two legs.
The quality of the positioning of the ventose is that you place the ventose first in a void area, and apply suction moderately: and do not prolong the positioning of the ventose, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the place equally: and do not cease from repeating this and continuing until you see the place redden and swell, and redness of blood appears: then scarify, and perform suction gradually. Then consider in positioning of bodies.
For one who is among humans of tender flesh and few pores, it is necessary to scarify him once, not more: so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And command the Wind-Letter (Ventosatori) to enlarge the incision and shallow it slightly; and temper the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is thickening in the blood, then it is necessary to incise it twice. In the first instance, an opening is made for subtle blood flow and its moisture; in the second instance, during the careful examination of deep blood extraction, thick blood is extracted.
If indeed the blood is extremely fetid, then an incision should be made for the third time, following it to the end.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood slowly or a little at a time, one incision is enough for us. If we wish to extract much blood, then we should make multiple incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make a deep incision; and the end of the incision is equal to its depth.

In the case where the skin is thick, hard, dark, and the pores are tight, it is necessary to anoint the wind-letter areas with open, lenitive, and resolvent oils; if it is during summertime, as is alkali oil, or oil of wormwood, or sweet almond oil, or oil from the seed of a squash.

For the one who has excessive thick ones, clean wind-diseased places with old wine, or with juniper water, or with dill boil, or chamomile: and similar to these.

Chapter on ventositation without scarification. Ventosations that are without scarification are those placed on the liver, spleen, podagra, stomach, navel, places of kidneys, and the ankle pit: because these members do not allow scarification upon them.
Nor does it seek through these, except the attraction of blood from one member to another; as we place ventosation on the breasts in the sickness of nasal bleeding. We make use of them to remove cold and offensive ventositation from one member by another; as we place ventosation on the stomach, and navel. For it rarefies the member, heats it up, and removes pain for that reason, because it has resolved that ventositation.
Also placed over the kidneys when there is pus or stone. For by its virtue, perhaps it opens up pus, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly when placed over the liver and spleen for the purpose of bleeding in both.
These ventosations are administered empty with incision only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered with warm water in the afflictions of pleurisy: and this is to be filled with a Ventose, and it should be large, hot water alone, or water in which herbs that agree have been boiled.
Form of the ventose administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingers wide, as we have formed it; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be on its side, in a place about halfway along it, a small hole, according to the size that the needle can enter; make it from brass or citron wood, with the edges rounded, equal, and smoothed; so as not to hurt the limb when placed upon it; and let there be in the middle of it a crossbar transverse from brass or iron, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this ventose is made large, larger than we have said, but not much smaller than it, according to the afflictions and age of the one who uses it.
For the ventoses of boys and those wasted are other than the ventoses of men and those with large bodies.
The quality of placing this ventose with fire upon a limb is to light a linen cloth with fire properly, or a small candle from tallow; and place it on the middle crossbar that is in the middle of the ventose; so that the fire may be lit about half-way down the ventose, so as not to burn the body of the sick person.
Then it is placed upon a limb; and the finger should be on the hole that I said, until we hold the ventose; while we need it, let us remove the finger, and steam will come out through the hole, and the ventose will immediately break.
Then, linen cloth according to its kind, and heat it if necessary.
In pneumothorax that is administered in the case of pleurisy with water, there is no bladder or hole; it is not administered except because it is filled with water and placed only on the member.
And this is its shape.
And this pneumothorax, as it is larger, to take in more water, is better.

Chapter II.
It is necessary to say at the beginning of this chapter the sum of speech and the headings, to which understanding is first required for the craftsman to his station regarding their truth; namely that you and those who are quick may know or learn this noble art from you, and others from you.
I say therefore that when fracture, dislocation, twist or fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary that you quickly go to him for bloodletting and resolving, or both at once; if not prohibited by something like weakness of strength; or he to whom this happens is a child, or old and decrepit; or the time is very hot or very cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, beans and heads; and prohibit wine, thick meats, and filling of foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until you are secure from a hot fever and do not fear effusion of matter to the limb; then let the weak return to his own regimen first, with which his accustomed practice was.
When the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing food in abundance, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity; such as food made from cooked wheat with water, rice, and heads, feet, and entrails of cattle, eggs, and fresh fish; also thick wine, and similar things. With this regimen, the healing of the fracture occurs more quickly, and better.
And know that the bones fractured in men who are in the first old age and worn out, it is not possible for them to be joined together and healed according to their nature always, because of the drying up of their limbs; they are consolidated and joined together which come from those at the end of softness, like the bones of small boys.
Nevertheless, nature connects something similar to glue around the broken bone from all sides, which has thickness, so that it adheres and tightens it until they are consolidated on both sides, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until it does not prohibit anything from its operations; and for this reason it is necessary to put nourishment for the sick man, which has thickness, viscosity, and heaviness, as we said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of limbs; since a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head of the bone; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the backbone; and similarly all other limbs exist differently from one another; and I come with the reminder of every type of fracture exposed in its own chapter distinguished from others. The species of bone fracture are diversified even within themselves.
Since the fracture of it is arched, furthermore stars occur in it. (Stars here refer to protrusions or irregularities in the broken bone.) And sometimes the fracture of it occurs along the length of the bone; and there are fractures called star-shaped, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as in a reed when it is cut with a knife to write. (Here, the analogy suggests that the fractured bone has protrusions like the fibers in a reed when it's cut.) And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with a wound and splitting at the head; and there is a small split fracture.
And every species has a skill or proper tool for its restoration according to its recollection, which is distinguished in its respective places.
And from that which the bone fracture is known, there is its crookedness, and its elevation; and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand. (Here, the description implies that a fractured bone may have an irregular shape, elevated edges, be visible or palpable, and produce a sound upon compression.)
When therefore there is no apparent crookedness in its location, nor a sound when you touch the bone with movement, nor does it find a weak great pain; then there is no fracture there, indeed it may be that it is a crookedness or a small split; therefore, you should not move it with your hand and compression altogether, instead place it on top of some medicine whose recollection comes to mind, after the appropriate time; then it is tied down gently. (Here, the advice suggests that if there's no apparent crookedness, sound upon touch or strong pain in a particular area, then it may not be a fracture. In such cases, the bone should be left undisturbed and treated with suitable medicine.)
And know that when a place arches and splits into two, furthermore stars occur in it, yet all parts are removed from their comparison; then it is necessary for you to begin at your hour for its correction, aligning it before an abscess of hot pus occurs on it. (This passage suggests that if a fracture splits into two and shows star-shaped protrusions, all the parts should be aligned for correction to prevent an abscess of hot pus from forming.)
If however a boil occurs warm, then let it be left for some days until the boil subsides. Then equal it accordingly to whatever method is possible by gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration of it, and its equalization, is easier than a bone, in which fractures occur.
Then bind it, according to when you will remember it.
If however there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from both parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand, if the part is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
Let your position be such that you place the member on a level surface according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try, by any means possible, to make those additions return, and in their places: and be careful according to your strength, lest pain or injury occurs due to your operation. And try according to your strength, to apply one of two bones with its companion, according to the best forms.
It is necessary at that hour that you test and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something abnormal there, correct it and equal it out according to the quantity of your power.
And beware of excessive extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For often they cause boils to occur by their operation, or contraction in a limb: as we have already seen this many times from their operations.
Then connect after the equalization and proper operation, and tightening of that limb, and peace, and calmness: and warn the weak against it. Make him take care that he does not move himself during the hours of his vigil, and during the revolutions of his sleep, and during his agitation, and during his digestion, and all his movements with utmost strength. Make him take care that there is no position of the limb which follows pain. This is because when he feels pain or injury in the position of a limb, then it is necessary to change him to another position from which he does not feel pain.
And be careful that his position is equal and straight; so that there is no twisting in the limb when it is restored.
The quality of the binding of a broken limb is as I have told.
Know that broken limbs are classified according to their smallness, size, and shape.
Those which are small, such as the arm, the finger, the wrist and similar ones, then it is necessary that there be a soft, fine wrapping of cloth around them. Those which are large, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary that there be a wide, hard wrapping around them.
Because a broad binding connects a large limb and binds it from all sides with an equal pressure so that no spot is left uncovered.
Then after your evacuation, begin to place the appropriate ointment on it on a soft woolen cloth over the site of the fracture. Then begin to wrap the binding over the site of the fracture three or four times, according to how deserving the limb is; and tighten your hand slightly with the binding.
Then go with him to the upper part above the site of the fracture and tighten it less than your previous binding which you had bound the broken place. Then stretch yourself slightly with a gentle pulling away from the location of the fracture, and gradually soften the stiffness, until you can grasp a large amount from a healthy place.
Then take another poultice and apply it again over the fractured location with more layers. Afterwards, go down with the poultice to the lower part below the fracture. And let your work be in the stiffness of the poultice and its softening, according to what was said in the first layer of poultice application.
Then place soft materials such as wool or cloth between the poultice layers to match the curvature of the fracture, if there is a curvature; and if not, do not put anything in that area.
Then apply another layer of poultice over it. Then place evenly arranged stars (small elevations) immediately on top of these poultice layers if there is no swelling or warm abscess in that area.
But if there is swelling or an abscess there: then place on it what sits the abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then bind tightly the evenly arranged stars (small elevations).
Let the small elevations be made from pieces of wide reeds that have been shaved and shaped wisely; or let them be made of wood from which cribra (cobble-stones) are made, such as pine or yew, or calingi (small wooden balls); and let the width of each elevation be in this shape and this same form.
However, the elevation that is placed on the fracture should be slightly larger and wider than the others; and the length of the elevation should be according to what is allowed by the size and smallness of the limb.
Then bind tightly over the elevations with another layer of poultice, according to the first binding. Then bind above with threads properly, as we have said concerning the stricture: and it is that your stricture be over the place of the fracture more: and as it is more elongated from the fracture, the stricture is smaller.
It is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be of soft linen.
Since if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish people putting their threads made from twisted cords of rough linen, it is a great mistake: because the stricture falls with them beyond its quality.
And again, fine threads do not suit: since you will not achieve with the stricture what you desire with them.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between one astel and another.
If a sick person is injured by the extremities of the astels after the stricture in healthy places: then place under them from soft wool or camel's hair wool until nothing is injured from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and it happens that the disruption occurs in the skin, I will come again with its reminder in the second chapter regarding its uniqueness.
And know that not every fractured limb needs to be bound with astels from the first day. Because a limb that is large should not have upper astels placed on it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the onset of the hot swelling.
This is the method of plasters, which the ancients used, that are placed over a fracture and dislocation.
§ Common emplaster method abbreviated, restoring the fracture, suitable for various temperaments, especially women and children: since it is from that which heat does not conquer nor cold.
And it is to receive from the flour mill dust and it is the marrow of the flour that hangs on the walls of the mill next to the millstone movement; and make it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white: and place its preparation not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another method for emplaster is for healing a fracture, and dislocation, and a twisting. Make almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath: and macerate all of them in ten parts of myrrh, and five parts each of aloes, and twenty parts of alethil: grind all of them and sift, and mix with water of alethil; or with cow's milk, if the bandage of the sick person is heated. Then use this emplaster. For it is of good composition, heals quickly broken bones and benefits many people.
Another method for emplaster also benefits a fracture and twisting. Make mugath, mess, white alteae, all of them in ten parts of myrrh, and five parts each of aloes, and six parts of acacia, twenty parts of Armenian pounded bolus: grind all of them with much grinding, sift, and make with water or cow's milk: and administer.
Another method for emplaster for joining together, and separated bones from their places: and eases the pain that happens to them, and splitting of the bone, and fracture. Take boiled wool, immerse it in vinegar and boiled oil: and place it on the spot.
And indeed, there is no virtue of healing in this emplaster, but it is good for the soothing of a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
Another method for emplaster that heals a broken bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig tree and wild papaver leaves: grind all of them, and make an emplaster from both.
Another short method for emplaster, administered for healing a broken bone: and when you wish the dissolution of a remaining abscess. Prepare a root of althea (Marshmallow), chamomile, flowers of violets, and a portion of the herb flour - all in equal parts: grind them all and make a paste with wine, unless the member is heated.
If it is heated, then make paste with water of coriander (Coriander) seeds or with water, and use it.
Another stronger plaster method for resolving a hard fistula when restoring a member. Prepare a root of althea (Marshmallow), flax seeds, boxwood, lentil, henna, wormwood, flowers of violets, and chamomile - all in equal parts: grind them all and make a paste with water of willow, or sweet water, or wine. Apply the whole thing according to the member's warmth and the calmness of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that it should be placed on a fractured member during its restoration, unless it is made of pitch and oil; nothing else; he said it should be of medium thickness between coarseness and subtlety.
Galenus, however, thought that these substances with the extraction of heat should be placed on a fractured member during its restoration, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and similar things from what we have mentioned.
Another plaster method that heals injuries and pain. Prepare milled (or very finely ground) figs, chickpeas, hair of man or feathers of bird, althea (Marshmallow), and salt - all in equal parts: grind them, sift them, and make a paste; then make a plaster from it.
The quantity of the bandage that should remain according to which it is necessary, then it is loosened, should be considered when there is no weak pain, itching, or moving of the fractured bone from its place; then do not loosen it for many days. If it happens to him at a place itching strongly, or distressing pain, or swelling: then hurry and dissolve (the plaster) in an hour of that (affected part), and remove the plaster from him: and take soft cloth or sea sponge, moist: and immerse it in warm water, and clean the place with it until the itching subsides, and the pain is relieved.
If it happens to him at a place itching strongly, or distressing pain, or swelling: then hurry and dissolve (the plaster) in an hour of that (affected part), and remove the plaster from him: and take soft cloth or sea sponge, moist: and immerse it in warm water, and clean the place with it until the itching subsides, and the pain is relieved. Then allow the member to rest for an hour. Then place over it soft wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind over it at night until you are secure from hot inflammation, and the swelling of the member subsides, and the pain is relieved.
Then repeatedly apply to it a narrow bandage and small plaster: and do not bind (it) with your first constriction and fine ones until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling and all the other symptoms have been completely removed: and you require plaster and bandage, then repeat this as you did in the first place equally.
If nothing of what we said happens to the member: then do not dissolve (the plaster) before three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released for 20 days according to what appears to you from the dissolution of the member, as we said, until when a fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh on it: add also in the bandage more than your first bandage completely: and add also in the thickening of food according to what was mentioned before its recall.
If you see the place of the fracture already dry out and become thinner than necessary: then know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it. Dissolve it (the embrocation) with warm water at every place where you apply it, and let it be in all three days, and it will slightly relieve the stricture.
Through this procedure, nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
What the foolish restorers do in place of a fractured member, when it is not restored as required, and they restore it according to its curvature, is an error due to their procedure, and there is great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have never seen any trace of theirs in this at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Chapter on the making of an incision in the head.
Types of fractures are diverse, and their shapes are many; and their causes are distinct.
For from a fracture is one that occurs by striking with a sword; and the strike removes bone completely up to the point where it reaches the siphac (a ligament beneath the skull), just as an ax does in wood, and for this reason this type of fracture is called "Axial".
Or the sword strikes some bone and cuts its surface but does not penetrate through the incision to the end; and this type of fracture is called "Kalan", that is, an Absolute Lever.
And a wound can be either of two types of fractures, large or small.
And from a fracture is one that is a crushing or rubbing; and its cause is a blow with a stone, a fall onto a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also one that penetrates, which is close to the panniculus (a layer beneath the skull), or it is on the surface of the bone; and this wound can be either of two types of fractures, large or small.
And from a fracture is one which is hidden within the bone at the subtlety of the hair; and it is a small crack, and for this reason this type of fracture is called capillary fracture.
