And there is a fracture which occurs by accident or striking with a stone, and similar things that cause a layer of the bone to enter within, making a hollow place; as happens to anvils when struck, and part of them enters within.
Indeed it happens most often in the wet bones of skulls, such as those of children.
And there are all these kinds of separating fractures, and I will mention them with the whole thing in its place.
We recognize all these kinds of fractures by examination on it and investigation of them with probing tools, and removal of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
The capillary type is recognized by this, that it appears on the bone and is scraped off, and it is limited with an encaustic iron; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, consider first the condition of the patient; if you see signs from his condition that are clearly indicative of fear, such as vomiting choler, extension, loss of intellect, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, discharge of the eyes, redness of them, and similar things among various conditions, it will probably not heal; and if you see signs that do not frighten you, and hope for recovery; then begin the treatment.
If a wounded man comes to you first with his wound; and this is during the days of winter, then it is necessary that you strive in bone removal before the 14th day according to all conditions.
If it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it corrupts what is under the bone from the fat, and such accidents as I have mentioned occur.
If the fracture of a bone has already reached the fat touching the brain and it is accompanied by small pieces and traction, then it is necessary that you cut the fragmented and smoothed part, as I have told you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded and to dig into the bone according to whatever method is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is light on what is weak.
If while digging into the bone there occurs a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then apply what is necessary to it. And it is necessary to fill the place with soaked rags in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be secure from bleeding. Then begin in the healing of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
The first method is to cut the bone with a sharp chisel: and this is its form.
Then administer another chisel wider than the previous one: and this is its form.
Then administer yet another chisel even wider than the second.
And the second method is to have a number of chisels of different sizes among you, some wider than others, and some shorter than others: and they should be at the end of sharpness, and made of iron or steel, and use them with ease when striking the chisel so as not to strike the head and injure it.
If the bone is hard and firm, then it is necessary that you bore around its circumference before you administer Incisoria, with Terebris which are named Terebra not penetrating too deep: and they are not called so unless because themselves do not pass beyond the bone's meaty limit to what is beyond it; therefore, because the Terebrum has a rounded extremity above that which is under its head and is sharp, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents immersion, and passing through the bone's thickness.
It is necessary for you to take a large number of Terebris from these, each one of which should correspond to the thickness of the bone, until you have all the Terebrum for that bone: whose sharp extremity's length and width should be in accordance with the thickness of that bone.
And this is the shape of three kinds of Terebrums or Trepans.
The quality of boring around the bone's circumference when it is broken, is: that you place the Terebrum on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the Terebrum to another place and put what is between every hole of that quantity or approximately.
Then cut with Incisoria what is between every hole of the bone: and make it as fine as possible on its edge, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with certain instruments which you have prepared for that purpose, such as tenaliae and delicate forceps.
And it is necessary to avoid all caution so that the Terebrum or Incisoria does not touch any part of the siphac.
When you are boring around the bone and it is separated from the siphac, if it is continuous with it and you extract it, then it is necessary that you grind and level its remaining extremity in the bone with another similar instrument like an Incisoria. It is necessary that it [the instrument] be thin and delicate more than other incisors.
If something remains from the small bones and cartilage: then take it with ease, with that which is at hand from your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with linen cloths and ointments as we said before.
And another method of operation is easy and entirely far from fear; which Galen said and praised greatly; and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin to expose the bone part from the place where the fracture is more violent and more apparent; until when you have exposed that part and placed under it the extreme end of this convex incisor, which has this form.
And the convex part, the convex does not cut anything; and the sharp part of it in its two sides going in length as you see: so that the convex part is attached to the ulna, with the sharp part incident on the bone.
Then strike upon the convexor with a small hammer until you incise the whole bone with ease, as it is revolved; and be careful that no cloth slips into what happens to slip which you fear completely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, and less ignorant of his craft, nor sleeping, then if something remained attached to the bone from a cloth in some places, he would cut it off with the extreme end of the convex incisor and free it with ease. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
If the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth and what is cut off from the bone is the face of it, and roughness and delicate cartilage remain in it: then it is necessary that you smooth down the roughness, and remove the delicate cartilage with subtle rasps which you have prepared in various quantities, so that it is possible for you to apply them more conveniently and correctly in every place according to what leads you the operation, and the fractured bone, and its figure. And it is necessary that you administer, according to your own judgment, first of all cutting the bone, more widely with those larger saws. Then use a finer one after that.
And it is necessary that you administer, according to your own judgment, first of all cutting the bone, more widely with those larger saws. Then use a finer one after that. And do not cease doing it continuously until you reach the point of administering thinner and finer [things], and subtler than all those [other things].
In each of the remaining small cracks and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to the correction of [them].
And there is a matter which is not hidden over all, to whichever pursuit this art has been devoted, and it stands upon that which we have written and shown concerning large fractures.
If, however, a piece of cloth is detected and retained when you are removing the bone: then it is necessary that you take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and dip it into rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another double or triple piece of cloth, and dip it into wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as possible above it, so that it is not heavy.
Then administer over it a broad bandage: and do not tighten it unless by the amount which holds the wound only: and release it with this care for one day or two days, until you are secure from hot infection. Then dissolve it and administer afterwards medicines with which excess dryness is needed: such as the roots of the garlic, and the flour of herbs, and sandalwood powder, and aristolochia, and similar [substances]: and there is that you make from these medicines a powder, which is to be sprinkled over the wound like dry ones.
And universally administer all medicine for the property of which it is for cleansing and not biting. Avoid, according to your skill, that the wound during your treatment is clean: neither be there in it dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness.
Avoid, according to your skill, that the wound during your treatment is clean: neither be there in it dirt from ointment, nor dirtiness. Do not allow pus to collect at all on it because when pus accumulates on a cerebral padding, it corrupts it and makes it rot, and after that it brings about great harm for the sick person: and already it has happened on the skull-bone, when the bone is being cleaned by him, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Then if the accidents that I have mentioned happen to the sick person, know that it is certainly dangerous.
And if blackness does not appear except from the medicine that is applied to it and the medicine has this property to make it black: then you must take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, and anoint it with these. Then place it on the skull-bone: afterwards take care of it with suitable means of healing until it is healed.
Restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except for two of its upper parts at once, or one of them: because there are two bones in it; since below it is cartilaginous, it does not break and it does not happen to it unless there is rubbing, and twisting, and curvature.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you must insert a small finger into the nostril and hold the broken part with your index finger and thumb on the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; this should be done skillfully with ease. And take care that pain does not happen to the sick person during your treatment.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and it does not reach that with the finger: then it is necessary that the end of the radius should be equalized, where there is a small swelling.
If the fracture occurs in two parts, then make it similar to that one: and approach with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day at a place of warm fumigation.
Then insert a piece of linen from a linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two pieces of linen if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be a swelling of such size in the linen that it fills the nostril opening.
And some of the old restorers said to immerse the linen pieces in butter and change them every day. But I do not see this, rather it is necessary that the linen piece should be immersed in egg white, prepared with miller's flour. Then release the linen, until the bone hardens and the cartilage thickens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose where the linen was, after soft cloth has been placed on top of it; and this holds the nasal fracture more strongly, so that a sick person may not be prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do with the linen pieces.
If the nose happens to develop a wound while operating on a warm fumigation, apply nasal plasters with tar, or with pitch softened in rose oil, or a little bit of alkali.
But if no warm fumigation occurs, then it is necessary that the plasters be applied with camel's flour and incense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft cloth upon him (the patient), and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken and fractured in a small way or crushed, then it is necessary that you cut open and extract them with suitable instruments. Then suture it (the wound), and take care of him with whatever that promotes healing, and consolidates it from suitable ointments or plasters for that purpose.
If there is a wound in the nose, then it is necessary that you take care of him with linen cloths: and administer a leaden cannula until it heals.
Chapter on Restoring the Lower Jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound associated with it, then examine whether its breakage is only on the outside and it is not fractured into two and curves towards the inside: in such a case, its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the breakage is on the right side, insert your left index finger into the patient's jaw: similarly, if the breakage is in the left jaw, then insert your right index finger: and push with it the curvature of the fracture towards the outside with ease using your other hand from the inner part of the bone correctly, and with equal force on the same side.
If the broken jaw is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer an extension from both sides according to straightness: until it is possible for them to be aligned equally.
If there has already been movement or disruption of the teeth, then tie together those that you hope will remain with a gold wire, silver wire, or silk thread. Then place a soft cloth over the broken jaw (mandible). Then place on top of him a double cloth, and place upon the cloth a well-made large pin or an equal piece of shoelather along the length of the mandible.
Then bind him above as you prepare his bandage and its application suits him until it does not break down; and instruct the sick one to rest peacefully and quietly; and place soft food for his loosening diet.
If you find that something has changed in his shape in any way: then hasten and cut him open on the third day. Then correct what has been distorted from it, and plaster him with flour of mill, with egg white or wheat flour after you have removed the purulent matter; and place a soft woolen cloth on top of the plaster.
While the aforementioned plaster remains on him and the bone arrangement does not change, leave him as he is, without cutting, until he is healed; or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then administer what I said many times for its reduction until the abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider. If the fractured part is separated from the bone or cartilage: then be subtle in this matter, so that you remove those fragments of cartilage with what suits you in their removal using tools.
If the wound opening is narrow: then widen it with a leech according to the quantity required by you.
Then when you remove the cartilage and there is nothing left of it, or if the wound opening is wide: and if it is not, place on top of it an appropriate plaster and let it heal until healed.
On the restoration of a fractured crossbar (Furcula).
The crossbar (Furcula) is most frequently broken from the front part near the humerus, and its fracture occurs in one of three modes.
Or it is broken into two pieces beyond which there occur protrusions; and this is easier for its restoration.
Or thus, when the protrusion occurs in the bone itself; and this is more difficult for its restoration.
Or it is broken with a wound.
And the operation in this case, when there is no wound, consists of making two assistants present; one to hold firmly the broken crossbar, and the other to stretch out the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form is achieved as required; and there should be no prominence or concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the patient's chin a sphere made of cloth or wool; and its size should be according to the quantity of your need; stretch and elevate the crossbar, and press the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as required.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the crossbar to the outside because it has reached deep; then the patient should lie on his back on his neck; and place a block under his shoulder in size, and press the assistant's arm towards the bottom until the bone of the crossbar is elevated from the deep to the top; at that point, correct the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a protrusion from the crossbar and it moves; then it is necessary to press down on it and extract the protrusion easily.
If it is retained in the bone, then use instruments prepared for incision of that kind, after you place under a lever the instrument which serves as a grip; and the instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a snail shell without a concavity.
And let its width be according to what you need for it due to the size of the bone and its part. But its length should also be as possible for you in surgery.
And it shall have two ends, as you see: one end shall be wide, and the other narrow.
If the fracture of the wound that you have cut when removing the splinter of bone is wide, and it is hot, then gather together two lips of the wound with stitching.
And if the fracture is small or you fear a hot abscess, then fill the wound with cloths and wool according to the size of the cut in the wound.
And if it happens that a hot abscess occurs, then pour cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine on it, and place it over it.
But the setting of bone without a wound and without an incision is, to place plaster on it made from flour milled with egg white. And put soft wool over it. Then place a sphere under its edge if needed. Then take the rotula (bloodletter), that is, a rod held by Arabs, which should be very long; and its width should be about one palm or nearly that. Then place doubled wool over the soft wool and the plaster. Then take an awl made of thin wood, whose width should be three fingers, and the same in length. Then roll her (it) in a cloth. Then roll the bandage with starch in the place of alumen that falls over the fracture and the wound. Then place alumen on the fracture as it is rolled, and roll it over its neck, and under the healthy titillicum and the weak one: and bring it back over all parts, so that you can see that the ligature holds the fracture tightly: and this is what should not be covered by you, and completely, so that the starch does not remove the splint from the fractured bone. And for this reason, it is necessary to examine the weak one every day. How often then does the bandage become soft and you see the starch already removed? Then correct it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the pillow of the sick one on his back, and put a small cushion under the healthy titillicum for him at night, so that with it he lifts up the assistance from the side himself. Then lift the fracture of the collarbone through lifting the shoulder.
Or bind the arm to the neck of the same; and do not loosen the bandage, unless it happens in the place that occurs from itching or sores, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add to the ligature and release it, until it heals and hardens.
The fracture of the collarbone is tightened and strengthened to a great extent for twenty days; and sometimes it happens in fewer in some men.
Chapter on the healing of the fracture of the spatula.
Rarely does the spatula break and in a wide place of it; and they do not break from it except for its edges.
When a place is broken from it and breaks in the middle of it, and it can only be known through touch: then prepare for the fracture according to its figure. Reduce it to its natural form by all means possible of your intellect.
Then place on the place powder from a mill with albumen of egg, and soft wax, and put on top a small piece of double-fold cloth. Then place over it a broad awl from thin wood according to the size of the spatula or slightly larger.
If there is any concavity under the awl in the spatula, then equal the concavity with soft wax until the awl descends according to equality.
Then bind above it with long alum strips, with a suitable tightening, and the awl's firmness, until it is not moved from its place.
Consider the binding every day. Whenever the binding becomes soft, bind it again, and equal the awl if it is moved from its place: let it lie weak on the healthy side.
The spatula should be restored in twenty or thirty days.
When this number has been completed, then loosen the binding: and you will be safe. For it is from the bones whose fracture is not feared nor their solution.
If it proceeds from a bone splinter and pierces below the skin: then cut through it, and remove it. And do as we said for a fracture of the neck of the scapula by hot cautery, if something happens from it.
Chapter on the restoration of breast fractures.
The chest is sometimes broken in the middle, and it happens rarely. The extremities of it break and are crushed due to its accidents.
If its middle part that tends towards the lower parts breaks and a severe pain occurs along with difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and perhaps blood is vomited, and a hollow appears on the broken bone which is not concealed to the senses, The restoration of it is for the weak to lie on his back and place a poultice between his spatulas.
Then press his shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides or flanks with hands: and grind it on an equal basis, in every possible way until it returns to the form of the bone, as is necessary.
Then place on him an emplaster and a stuffing, and place above it a star-shaped piece of thin material made from willow or sandalwood, or similar materials wrapped in cloth. Then grind it on a binding over the broken bone so that it is not displaced: and go with the bindings according to its roundness towards the back several times, and tie it with an appropriate suture.
Then consider the bindings in every hour and whenever it softens, tie it: and if necessity requires dissolution of it due to itching or corrosion that occurs at the place, or pain, or abscess, then hurry and dissolve it, and remove the poultice, and correct whatever has occurred with the method of its treatment.
Then return the poultice if you see it facing towards that: and attach a suture until it heals.
Chapter on Restoration of Ribs when they break.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in thick places following the back and from their extremities from the front part. It does not happen to them (the joints) by friction, unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it (the part) is not hidden from the sense during investigation with fingers.
And its restoration is made thus: so that it aligns the fracture with fingers according to the possible method, until it becomes equal in form, as required. Then plasters, and tie the broken bone with a bandage, if necessary.
If the fracture is oblique towards the inner parts (of the chest): then a severe pain occurs in the weak, and puncturing, such as the puncturing that occurs to one who has pleurisy; because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of blood frequently: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have been skilled in this with many skills; and among them are those who say that one should place food for the weak which generates inflammation and flatulence, so that the stomach is inflamed, and expanded, and pushed against the outside.
We, however, abhor to make a rapid cause for making a hot abscess, if it does not occur. If, however, it has already occurred, then one adds it (the hot abscess).
And some of them say: place a flatulent substance on the place, then squeeze it forcefully, and it is more like reasoning. Nevertheless, it is feared that it attracts excessive flatulence to the place, due to the disposition of weakness.
And some of them say: the place must be covered with wool that has already been soaked in hot oil; and little lice should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled (the abscess); so that the bandage is equal when one folds it according to roundness.
Then the sick person shall be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the illness presses heavily upon the sick person and causes pain, or if we pierce the skin with a lancet, causing injury, and we fear for him: then it is necessary to divide at the site, and expose the broken rib. Then we shall place upon it the instrument which we have mentioned before (the cautery called sifac), and cut through the bone with ease, and cut the same.
Then we shall bring together two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures: and treat the same with plasters, until it heals.
If a warm abscess should occur during the interval: then apply hot cuppings, and pour powdered almonds into warm oil, and place it on the site: and treat the sick person with that which is suitable for an abscess.
In addition, a cushion shall be placed upon the side on which he lies until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the fracture of the Spine and Neck.
Fracture occasionally occurs in the bones of the neck, and rarely happens to them attrition; similarly, in the spondylous of the back. When this occurs to anyone and you wish to know if it will heal or not: then consider (or examine). For if you see that his hands have already become softened, stunned, and dead; and he cannot move them, extend them, or contract them; and when you press them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor does he feel pain in them: then you shall know that it will not heal, and in many dispositions it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels the constriction with fingers and prick (or pain) in them: then you shall know that the nucha bone is safe, and that the weak one will heal with treatment.
If this occurs in the spondylous of the back and you wish to know if it will heal or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they have become softened, and this happens to them as it happened to the hands: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and constipation occur without will; and when he lies on his abdomen, flatulence occurs without will; and when he lies on his back again, wishing to urinate, but cannot: then you shall know that it is mortal. Do not labor in the treatment of him (i.e., do not treat him).
If it does not happen to him anything from that, the case is lighter.
And the treatment for what happens to him from that is to apply warm oil (or fat) on the affected spondylous; thus you shall place warm oil only on the rubbed spondylous, or with goose grease saturated ointment.
Place it repeatedly upon the same [wound] daily until the abscess becomes warm: then place upon the location one of the soothing and draining plasters, and bind it over with a ligament: and instruct the sick one to use quiet and tranquility. Let him not sleep on the side where he has found pain, until it is healed.
If it should occur due to the dislocation of the hip bone or something that has already separated from it: then it is necessary to sever it through the skin, and remove it. Then gather together two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture. Then take care of him with some meaty substance, from ointments, until he is healed.
But if the last bone of the alhausos is broken and the end of the tail is: then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and with the other hand align the broken bone as much as possible, according to what is possible, and in the second alignment.
Then place it on top of it and remove it: and take care of the wound with the treatment that preceded it, until it is healed.
Restoration of an ankle.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the ankles rarely break. If the bones break, then their fracture is not anything other than a comminution at the ends of them; they are split lengthwise and bent towards the interior; and it occurs in the sick man that there is pain in the location and puncturing; and the leg which breaks becomes numb.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until you stand over the fracture in the manner of its figure.
If the fracture is only at the ends of it, then align the broken part with that which is prepared for you from alignment, until it resembles its natural figure.
If a fracture is in length or already inclined towards the inside parts, then lay the weak one on his stomach, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have matched it (the fracture), place plaster on it; then place a splint made of wood or hide over it and tie a bandage above it where you fear neither displacement of the fracture nor removal of the splint.
And make the concavity of those (the bones) match with that which fills it, until the bandage fits it equally; and command the weak one to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If, however, it happens to him (the patient) that a hot abscess occurs: then cease from extending it and its treatment, until the hot abscess settles; and place what he sits on over it, as was before, and then return to its treatment; and tie it as necessary.
If, however, it happens to the bone that it is gnawed or crushed, extract (the foreign object).
Chapter on the Restoration of Fractures (of Adiutorius)
Assistance is that which extends from the elbow to the head of a spatula.
If it breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One method is that you take a flat piece of wood of medium size in the following shape.
And tie two ligaments at both ends.
Next it is suspended from a raised place: and the sick one sits on the chair. Then he throws his broken arm upon a log until it reaches the notch of that log, where the crack of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above him to stretch the patient below. Then the physician sets the fracture with his hands until it returns to its proper place.
Another method is for the sick one to lie on his back: and his hand is suspended from his neck with a rope. Then command two attendants to hold one of them above the fracture with their hands: and let another hold him below, while each one stretches his part.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then tie something under the fracture with a rope, and above it with another: and each of the two attendants stretches his part to its respective side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then a ligament must be placed under the notch and another one towards the elbow joint.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then a ligament must be placed above that place and another one on the elbow.
Then align the fracture easily without strain until it is equal according to what is required and properly applied: then bind it if no hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding for seven days. Place a piece of linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil on him until the abscess subsides: then bind it: and the method of binding is to apply a plaster over the fracture.
Then place the inversion from new cloth upon the plaster. Then gather the arm upon the help itself, and place his open hand upon his own shoulder.
Place the cloth and bind upon the help of the arm so that the arm remains at the position of stars: if it does not forbid you by something, nor be changed by anything from a broken bone.
If you fear that something will be changed by a broken bone, then use stars: and there is one, so that you place upon the fracture itself a star which is wider and stronger than other stars. And place the width of each star at the length of a finger: and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from every side.
Then bind upon the stars the ligature which I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And there is that your ligature should be stronger over the place of the fracture. And as much as the fracture is prolonged, the ligature should be less.
If therefore you see the position of stars and ligature, as we said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you fear a hot abscess, then release the ligature and stars until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the ligature in all three days, so that it does not happen at the place an itch, or swelling, or food is forbidden to reach the limb, because of the position of excessive ligature. Correct therefore this whole thing, as we said.
If indeed you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not release the ligature, unless after three days or more.
And let the rest of the sick one be on his back and his hand upon his stomach: and place under the help a blanket full of wool equally.
Consider at every hour of the night or day, so that the shape of the broken member is not loosened, nor the ligament softened. Correct it all with your power.
Then place the food for the sick according to the order that we have commanded, so that it is a subtle food at first, until the bone begins to tighten; then it should be made to grow thicker for him.
For it is the custom of helping and healing a leg that it should be tightened for forty days; then, therefore, it should be loosened, and a bath should be administered, and it should be treated with ointments which correct it.
If the fracture is foul, scrape it; then do not loosen the ligament and the splints from it for fifty days, and until two months.
Chapter on the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two fore-arm bones.
One of them is small; and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed below the small one from the lower part.
Therefore, perhaps only the larger fore-arm bone is broken, or perhaps both are broken.
When the small fore-arm bone above is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower fore-arm bone is broken, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken simultaneously.
If the bone that is broken is the small fore-arm bone above, then it suits the doctor in its restoration, that he puts on an extension smaller with ease, until it equals itself.
But if the large fore-arm bone is the one that is broken: then it is necessary that a strong bandage be put on.
It is necessary that [the figure of] the hand should be placed [there] at its restoration, and [its extension] extended upon the pulley. The thumb should be higher than all the fingers, and the lower joint [of the thumb] should be higher than all the other [joints or fingers], or what is left of them: and the weak one sitting square on himself, and let the pulley be before him, or in front of him, at a height so that he does not strain himself.
Then, let the servant extend the arm from its lower part, with his hand, or with a ligament. And another servant extends it likewise from above.
Then the doctor equalizes [or adjusts] the bone until he can bring it back to its best shape possible.
If there are shards [or fragments] of bone in the fracture, then try to reduce all the fragments to their respective places by your own effort.
But if it appears that a shard of bone is separated and pierces the skin and there is no hope for its restoration, then plug it over and remove it according to the method we have mentioned earlier.
But if there is a fracture with a wound, I have already written a separate chapter about it, therefore take its treatment from that point onward.
If at the beginning of your restoration a hot abscess occurs, then take linen cloth made with beeswax mixed with rose oil and white wax, and let it be of medium thickness between thickness and subtlety: and bind the cloth over it tightly with a thin string until the abscess settles down: then remove the beeswax, and place an ointment prepared from milled flour and egg white. Then place the awls [or metal splints], and let the awl that is placed over the fracture be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of awls for the arm is six in several arrangements: whether it is a fracture in one splint, or in two splints at once.
Then place your tie (ligature) over the stronger and more prominent part of the fracture, and a little farther towards the upper part of the fracture, or towards its lower part: or place a softer ligature on it, as previously remembered in the beginning of the chapter.
The bandages that cover the fracture should be soft, damp: they should not be hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen and medium between fine and coarse, as we have said.
Consider the limb (body part) and ligament after days (time has passed). If something happens that requires rectification, such as itching on the limb: then it is necessary to apply warm embrocation to the limb with hot water until the itch subsides, and leave the limb unbound until it subsides, then tie it again.
If the ligature has been softened and the bone removed, and similar things have happened: then correct it all with your art, and consider again.
Because if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to the excess of the ligature, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and leave it for some days until it reaches its nourishment, then tie it again.
But if nothing of what we have mentioned happens to the sick (person), then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or around that time.
Then the hands of the sick one are suspended on his neck. And let his neck be straight, and keep it from unnecessary movements with your art. Make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is restored in thirty or forty days; and it may be restored in twenty-eight days. The whole thing is according to the dispositions of humors and the dispositions of virtues.
On the restoration of broken bones in hands.
Know that the breakage of the hand and the joints of fingers happens rarely. And it does not happen to him unless he is rubbed repeatedly.
When a foot breakage occurs or by rubbing, then it is necessary that the lame man sits on a stool; and before him there should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on it extended.
Then the servant should spread out the broken bones, and the doctor should adjust them until they join together well with good union.
Then it is necessary that he applies plaster and wool, if there is no hot abscess occurring. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place; and you already wound it in linen cloth.
If the breakage is in the lower part around the heel of the foot, then make a similar ball from cloth; and order the lame man to bend over it with his broken foot; then it should be wrapped with a long piece of cloth.
And the splint should be made of calfskin, with softness so that it can be applied to the body of the foot; and it should be wrapped according to what is necessary.
If the breakage is on the outside, then it is necessary that you place a splint on top and another one from below on the foot; and keep the hand open and straight. Then put a bandage, as the hand is rolled up; and extend it between the fingers with a band.
If a breakage happens to any of the joints of fingers, then if it is the thumb, adjust as necessary. Then wrap with the foot; and if there are fingers, place a straight splint under it, smaller; so that the breakage is lifted up and not moved.
If the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, index, ear or ring finger, then it should be equalized and tied with a healthy finger that follows it; or they may all be tied according to continuity, and this is better. Place on top of it a smaller splint, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration and after its arrangement, the application of warm poultices; and apply what is necessary when something happens from it, according to its repeated narrative.
Chapter on the restoration of a hip fracture.
The hip bone is broken often and becomes apparent to the senses; because it is turned towards the inner and posterior parts.
And its restoration is made by tying a ligament above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. The sick one lies on his face.
Then stretch out every ligament serving to its part according to equality; and this is when the fracture is in the middle bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let a ligament be to the side of the pelvis so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let a ligament be near the knee so that the extension falls to the lower parts.
Then the physician equates the bone with both hands of his, until he reduces it to its natural shape; and it unites the bone with a good union.
Then it is necessary that you place an emulsion and a tight bandage if there is no abscess in the member. If an abscess occurs there: then leave it for some days until the abscess settles down warm; then return to your treatment.
Stricture it accordingly. You should wrap a hard linen around the fracture; that is, bind it: and double it twice, or thrice: and let there remain an excess of it. Then wrap the leg until you reach the heel at the root of the toes.
Insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper parts of both sides. Then roll the excess on the leg and hip.
Then place stars on the hip at the site of the fracture itself, and place one star on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the voids between the leg and hip with soft cloths to make the compression equal.
Then begin with strong compressions from the middle of the fracture site with three or four wraps. And as you loosen the bandage from the fracture site, let your compression be less and softer, and gentler.
Then turn to both ends of the thread that you inserted between the hip and the leg. Tie three stars on each end of it. Then go to both ends of the thread to the bottom, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with each end, also tie the ends of the stars on the opposite side so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then release the compression on that side until it no longer causes the member to rot, or develop an abscess, swelling, or similar things. If something happens from there. Approach it again with the resolution of that matter, and correct entirely whatever happens from there according to what we said many times before.
But if a splinter is in the bone that pierces it: then it is necessary to remove it equally, if it is possible for you. If not, find on it and remove it; and take care of the one who preceded it until it heals.
When this hip fracture is tightly bound with splints, as mentioned in the adjuvant and arm chapter, it should be kept immobile until it has healed.
Nevertheless, in healing such a fracture, lameness does not occur for the weak. If it is healed alone, except that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary for the one who has it, to lameness always.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days or it is too little or too much; all of this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter on healing a knee-joint fracture.
Know that the knee joint rarely fractures but it often suffers from attrition.
But if it happens to be a fracture, then it is not otherwise than a crack or hole in its parts. And it occurs with an injury, and without an injury. It stands on that whole part with sensation.
Its healing is as follows: it should be made to fit together the parts that were broken with fingers until it grows and joins together according to what is possible from adjustment and ease, and construction.
Then put a plaster made from barley flour or milled flour with egg white on it; and place over it a round splint, if needed; and bind above it with a suitable splint.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have mentioned in other fractures; such as a hot abscess and similar things; so that you counter every occurrence with what suits it until it heals.
Chapter on healing a leg-fracture.
Know that the leg consists of two bones, one is large and called the Leg: and the other small and called Ankle.
And it often happens that a fracture of this kind occurs in both legs, as it does in two bones of the arm; and for this reason the repair is made equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken, the leg will bend in all directions.
And if the small bone is broken, the leg bends towards the front.
If the large bone is broken, you will find it lower: and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, equalization, and ligature of the stars equally.
Nevertheless, if the fracture of the leg is a foul one, with many seething, you should have lesser and gentler extension.
In the leg indeed there is something more than in the arm during the operation: and it is that when you equalize the stars and complete your entire operation, then take two bands made of pine wood, which are used in the manufacture of solariorum placed under tables; and they are made from the blades of the palm; and choose from them those with a small thickness, and neither be too thin; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then fold over one cloth with two folds according to its length. Place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the other side: and let it be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two bands in three places at both ends and in the middle. For since compression is forbidden, the leg will bend to the right and left, and stand with good posture.
And indeed, the cradle should be made of wood in the length of the leg, and the leg should be placed in it to keep it from moving.
And indeed, it is necessary that you make this for a fracture when there is a wound with it.
Then consider the leg every day and strive to keep it free from gangrene, swelling, or any other ailments. If any of these occur, then apply to it whatever is necessary until it heals, if God wills.
On the fracture of bones in the foot and fingers.
The calcaneus bone does not usually break completely.
Fractures of the bones in the foot occur sometimes, and fractures in the fingers are rare as well; they do not usually happen to them unless they are rubbed in multiple positions.
If a fracture of the bones in the foot occurs and you see that the bones are lifting up above each other, then place the injured foot on the ground prepared as when walking.
Then stand and place your foot on the elevated bone; afterwards press or tread on it; and keep it even, until it returns to its original position.
Then apply ointment and plaster on top; place a small table under the heel of the foot with two flat heads.
Then bind it with an appropriate strap after you have wrapped it with linen cloths and whatever else is needed.
When three or four days have passed, then unbind the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned if they are fractured or dislocated. Therefore, treatment is done as you see it.
If some fingers are broken, then restore them and align them as I have told you for the restoration of fingers on the hand. Then place your finger as long as the digit, and let it be a little wider; then place under the foot this plank that I told you, and bind it with an appropriate suture.
If what is fractured consists of two or more bones, then place a plaster of the appropriate size over each digit, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the plank below the foot; let it be standing on one head of the plank outside the abdomen of the foot to hold it firmly.
And it is appropriate for you not to forget that you should consider everything that I told you in the other fractures, which we have mentioned; and apply each case with what is appropriate.
Section on female facial fracture, clavicle bone, and male pelvic fracture.
When a woman's face is fractured, then make her sit in a square position. Then lower her to the back part of it slightly and hold her backward.
Next, let the midwife fill the woman's wound with a linen cloth until it is filled; and make the wound on her face like a sphere.
Then the woman should be shaken and her back gradually flattened. For that linen cloth will come out until it is made before the door of the wound like a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a poultice on her back. When she wants to urinate, let her remove the linen cloth easily, then cleanse; and return it in the manner by which you first inserted it; then return to the poultice first.
Let that be done for seven days or about, because the bone itself will heal.
And if you wish, take the bladder of an animal, bind it over its opening with a reed cannula, and insert the entire bladder into her wound. Then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated within the cavity, for the fracture will return.
Then fill it with a padding and let it remain for some days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when the skullcap of a man or woman breaks, administer to its restoration and apply what we have said about Ancha's bone.
Do not hide the rectum in these nearby fractures, which seldom occur, especially if one has some knowledge or experience.
Understand that my book is much, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many ways the course of the rectum and its constriction in its restoration and healing; therefore understand it.
In a man's penis when it breaks, take the goose's throat: insert the penis into it. Then place on top of it a covering made of cloth and wrap it up, let it rest for three days or around that time, and it will heal if God wills.
[Chapter Title] On the restoration of bone fractures when there is a wound.
It is appropriate for one who has a bone fracture along with a wound, especially if it is large like the hip bone or arm bone, to rush and immediately call for bloodletting; if the conditions of bloodletting are attested to you as we have said.
If the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you rush to its cutting, spreading powder on top of it as much as possible if no other remedy is present for you.
Then begin to repair the fracture on the same day; do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
If an abscess hot, then delay the repair of it until the ninth day, until the abscess settles down. And do not approach him on the third and fourth day, because you would cause bad accidents to happen to him.
If the broken bone projects above the skin, then it is necessary to pull it back and align it with your hands easily and slightly extend it.
If the extension is not prepared and alignment by your hands, then reduce it with this instrument. And the tool is made of iron, whose length is the size of seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound. Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the amount needed for that in any kind of healing in all types of fractures.
And it should be round; and there should be a small thickness to avoid doubling under pressure on it during the operation hour; and its tip should be sharp, with an edge at its ends. And the surface of it should be broad from the middle to the bottom.
This is the form of it.
It is necessary in this operation that the tip of it should be sharp and rounded on top of the protruding bone; and push it with one thrust until the bone returns and aligns itself in some way.
Try to align the extremities of the fracture against each other.
If the extremities of the broken bone are delicate and do not accept the tool well, then it is necessary to cut the edge of that bone until the tool has power over it.
If you cannot pull it out as we have explained altogether, then cut it with what is suitable for him from the incisions which we mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in any way that is possible for you.
Then file down what remains on the bone from its roughness and small layers.
When the bone is pulled out and if it becomes weak after being pulled back and causes a violent pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this. For by doing so, you confer great aid upon the weak, when you complete the restoration that you do to the bone.
Then immerse a linen cloth in wine steeped in black. And properly if it is during summer. And do not place on the wound any green substances or anything containing oil; so as not to cause rot and decay at the wound site.
Then administer astilas at the hour of your restoration of the bone. And let the wound heal; thus, you will cut with forceps the opening in the inflammation according to the size of the wound.
And beware with all caution, that you do not squeeze the wound when it is broken. For many times have foolish physicians done this; and they have caused death or decay or gangrene, that is, blood poisoning. And let your ligature be soft, gentle, diverse from the other fracture ligatures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear certain evil accidents which we have mentioned, and find pain at a suspended location: then it is not necessary to place astilas on it. And make incisions from hard cloths in the place of astilas, and tighten it with them.
When, however, it is one day or two days later, and you see that the wound begins to produce pus: then remove from above the linen cloth which you had placed on it with wine. Then apply linen and ointments according to our custom for treating wounds, such as four-ingredient ointment (tetrapharmacon) and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you, that you loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every day at morning and evening until it heals and cures.
It is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way as to allow the pus to flow out from it easily towards the lower part.
If many days pass without the wound healing or pus being expelled from it: then you will know that there is a small fracture of the bone there. Therefore, you should examine the wound with pressure. As for the number of small fractures separated, remove them and extract them. And those that are not separated and pierce the limb, causing pain, try to cut them and remove them in any way possible for you.
If there occurs a gangrene or suppuration, that is, putrefaction and corruption: then it is necessary to apply to each one of them what is suitable for its treatment, whose treatment was previously described in the corresponding chapter.
And from the discussion that I have made so far and to which you should give your full attention when a bone is broken and protrudes from the limb, such as the hip or shoulder bones, and similar large ones: do not presume to set it yourself, for frequently death occurs from this; rather let it be until it becomes putrefied.
Perhaps it falls by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then care for the wound if it seems to you: and if not, let it be.
On the treatment of a nodule on a fracture's track.
This frequently occurs as a knotting in the process of healing a fracture. Primarily, what is near the joints: it becomes ugly due to this shape of the member. And possibly prevents the member from its natural movement.
Therefore, consider if the knotting is recent: administer to it medications that have a stiffening effect, such as aloes, olibanum, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar ones.
Thus, take some or all of these: and mix them with stiffening wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the knotting in a plaster, and bind it well with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage, do not loosen it for many days. Then unbind it, and bind another one until the knotting is removed.
Or bind well over it a leaden plate. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the members.
If the knotting has already turned into stone and is hardened and urgently calls for its removal: then apply a plaster from above it, and cut off the excessive projecting part: or file it with certain rasps until it is removed: and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter on treatment when the member remains subtle after that, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored and the member remaining after it is subtle and weak, it is not done except for many reasons. One of the reasons is that there is a large amount of disintegration of the ligament and its binding not as required: either due to excess restriction of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from flowing to the member: or due to excessive embrocation at an inappropriate time: or due to a small amount of blood in the body of the weak person, and its debilitation.
The care for this is to feed the weak person, and make his body tender until blood multiplies in him. Administer a bath. And pour joy and gladness over him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that it attracts much nourishment to itself. And anoint it with embrocation and warm water until the nourishment runs, and returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored twisted.
If a limb already restored after its healing becomes twisted and the bone of the fracture protrudes or knots, and it is disfigured by that part of the limb, but the limb does not forbid its natural operation, then one should not receive the speech of that person who thinks that the limb must be broken again.
And there have been many fools among doctors and restorers who did this on our land.
And this operation is indeed to be greatly condemned, leading to death or a great fear that it might be deadly.
But if limping and knotting are recent: then it is necessary to anoint with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves of elder, its root, and honeysuckle, and similar ones. And let it be made as an ointment with a softening plaster, as ditchulon properly made.
Let the mucilage of the root of the marshmallow be taken, and grind it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and let it be applied with that.
Let figs be taken and ground with pigeon dung and similar ones which are named among the medications that diminish consolidation.
And sometimes a knot is loosened with constant and gentle rubbing, which is done with moist hands. Let motion be applied to the member in every hour (of the day).
If, however, the twist has already occurred and it has become strong, and has turned into a stone, and there is necessity for its treatment with iron: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuation of the bone be loosened, and what remains superfluous from the knot or the bone with chisels be cut away: and let ease be applied to it with diligence and care.
Chapter on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joints from its place. Why it should be prevented from moving and why it becomes deformed in appearance of the member. And causes severe pain to occur on top of the weak, and violent injuries.
When therefore a dislocation occurs, then it is necessary to hasten for its restoration; nor should it be delayed at all.
For if it is delayed, the place may recede, and possibly with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it should not be moved nor extended in the hour of its relocation. Because many times it causes spasms and painful injuries.
However, when that happens, then it is necessary to rush for the bloodletting of the weak. Then it should be allowed to rest until the abscess has subsided.
Then broom the limb with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced easily.
And every part of the body should be treated with it, when its remembrance comes to its place.
I myself have already ordered chapters on dislocations, even according to what was previously discussed in the context of fractures, from the top part of the body downwards.
Chapter on the treatment of lower jaw dislocation.
Two mandibles are scarcely dislocated except rarely.
Dislocation of both [occurs] according to two ways. For either they are moved slightly from their places and softened slightly, or they are separated with great force. Or the dislocations are complete and integral until they are softened up to the breast, until the saliva of the sick man runs out and he cannot hold it nor close his mouth, and his tongue is rolled up with speech.
When the dislocation is slight, then it returns by itself in various positions with scarcity.
If the dislocation is complete and integral, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly and without delay: and there should be someone to hold the head of the sick man; and the doctor should insert his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated mandible, if the dislocation is on one side. Or two thumbs are inserted at the same time, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, as he sees fit.
And command the sick man to clean his mandibles and to wash them for eating on every side. The doctor pushes the mandible and forces it back to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both mandibles are at the same time, then administer steam with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay the reduction of both, as we have said.
When they return and come together and the sick man's mouth is closed, and they are not softened: then place on them small pieces of cloth, with wax that has been made from beeswax; and rose oil.
Then it should be tied easily with soft ligatures.
And let the sleep of the sick man be on his back: and let his head be supported between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right and to the left: neither let him try or be forced to chew something: rather, let his food be made soft, until when the pain is removed, and the jaw is loosened: then let him eat whatever he wants, and let this be administered easily.
Let him not strain on the operation of his mouth during eating and drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is loosened and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places: then fever often occurs, and continuous vomiting: and perhaps the sick man's abdomen bursts open, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this: then you will know that it is a fatal event, and he who experiences this often dies within ten days.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocation of the neck and the limb of the shoulder.
The neck is not dislocated from within on the side, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside: and this can be felt.
And its restoration is that the sick man lies on his back and stretches out his arms. Then bend your hand into a fist with a strong grasp: for it will return.
Then place the plaster and pillows on top of it, and bind it tightly.
The limbs following the elbow, which are connected to it, are not usually dislocated, unless rarely. If it is dislocated on some day: then it must be reduced and set in accordance with what we have said, and as you will prepare for yourself.
Then place on it [the wound] the plaster, and the leeches, and the suture. And tell the sick person, to hold fast to tranquility and rest, until he is healed.
When this same treatment brings about the relief of the shoulder's extremity, even if it also separates from its place.
Chapter on the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder.
You should know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of them is when it dislocates to the palmar side towards the lower parts. The second one is when it dislocates to the chest area. And perhaps it may be dislocated towards the upper part of the humerus; and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back side, because of the spine (of the scapula). And it is not dislocated to the front side, because of the nerves.
Mostly it is indeed dislocated and exits towards the palmar side; especially in those whose flesh is scanty. Since it goes out quickly and enters quickly (back into place).
But in those whose flesh is plentiful, it is just the opposite, namely that it goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty (back into place).
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow or an accident and the humerus is pushed out by a blow with something hot, and they think this because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you must try that out until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
The dislocation is known when it is towards the palmar side; and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. For you find among them an obvious difference, and in the head of the humerus you find a depression, and under the palm of the hand at touch, it seems like an egg; and an invalid cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards; then you find a clear feeling that is not concealed.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent or if the child is weak.
And its reduction is, that he lifts his assistant's hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his thumb and lift the joint firmly upwards to its place; and the assistant lifts his hand, and extends it upwards. Then he puts it down below, for he quickly returns (to its position).
If it does not go back as we have said, and from the beginning to where dislocation occurs there are many days; then it is necessary that the sick one be bathed in warm water, and treated with an embrocation which softens and soothes, such as if they decoct root of althea, and fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and it (the embrocation) is administered.
Then the sick one lies down on his back; and a sphere and wool are to be placed under his thumb which is equal between softening and tightening.
Then the doctor places his heel on the sphere, and lifts the head of the arm with force, and draws the hand of the sick one downwards.
And if you wish, do so according to this method; and it is that you make a man who is longer than the sick one present, who stands from the side, and intrudes his arm under the thumb of the sick one: and lifts the thumb upwards, until the sick one is suspended in the air. And another assistant pulls the hand of the sick one downwards towards the stomach of the same. If the sick one is light: then it is necessary that something else is suspended with him to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately (to its position).
And it is also reduced according to another method; and it is that a long wooden stick is formed on the ground, whose head is round-shaped like a mitten of the deadman's arm (mortarer's arm), which is not too thick nor too thin. Then, place it under the sick one after the logs of soft wood have been placed on his head; and let the sick person stand according to the length of the logs.
Then his hand is to be extended towards the lower part from one side; and his body is to be extended from the other side with force; for the joining [of bones] returns to its place quickly.
If it is difficult to reduce [the fracture] by all the means we have mentioned, then employ this remedy: you shall take a log whose length is equal to two forearms and its width is four fingers, and its thickness is that of two fingers; the end should be rounded so as to widen the entrance into the deep socket [of the joint], in this manner.
Then, soft cloths are placed over the rounded log to prevent injury to the sick log.
Then, it is to be placed under the injured joint so that his hand or arm can be extended over the log until reaching the lower part; and the log is to be bound to the help [the other log], arm, and end of the hand.
Then, place his arm on a step of the ladder [the other log] by its width; and extend his hand towards the lower part; and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. For the joining [of bones] quickly enters.
When the entrance is completed in whatever manner possible; then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size under the injured joint [the other log]. Then place a prepared plaster made from milled powder with balm and albumen of egg white over the entire shoulder as it is rotated above.
Then tighten a bandage under the injured joint; and rotate the strap over the plaster above; and connect his hand to the neck of the same [person]; let it be, without moving his hand for seven days.
It is necessary that food be sparse until the limb recovers, for it heals more quickly.
Then let it (the dressing) be larger after seven or five days, and let it be shaken with motion. If it hardens and is not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is repeatedly dislocated due to the moisture that occurs or for another reason: then it is necessary to administer cautery with three holes, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
If this is done and the ligament is dissolved after seven days and the joint is not hardened and the emplaster and compression are not applied to it several times nor does it become hard and falls and cannot be raised towards above: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus have been cut or stretched or softened: and you know that the joint will never become fixed in its place.
For a dislocation towards the chest and breast or reduction to the rear is done with pressure and extension using hands until it returns, and then further compression and care are applied until it heals.
If pain or slowness of movement occurs after healing in the limb, then the sick person should use baths many times until that pain is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter on the treatment of the elbow joint.
You should know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty as well; and it is indeed dislocated in all directions, but particularly towards the front or the rear.
And you will know that this dislocation is not hidden from you; because it falls under sight and touch, whichever way it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is made apparent to you. For indeed the joint will be concave and the arm cannot double, nor can it touch its own shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and immediately reduce the dislocation, before a hot abscess occurs.
For if a hot abscess occurs, the reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For indeed, every form of dislocation is worse and more painful; and many times death follows with it.
And the restoration of it, when it can be reduced, is that the hand should be extended with two hands; and the arm should be expanded, and the hands of the physician should be above the elbow and below it, and he himself should push the joint with the tips of his fingers or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is towards the front: then it returns in such a way that it doubles the hand with its own, until it strikes the elbow which is dislocated with the root of its palm.
But if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a strong and violent extension; and it is that two attendants extend the arm; and they should hold him also so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is turned to every side after it has been wrapped around its own hand with a double cloth or wide bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he binds it with oil; so that it is from those things which aid in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then he pushes the joint with a strong push until it returns.
After the reduction [of the fracture], it should be placed on top of it a plaster with styptics and cauterization with the white of an egg; the stricture should be tied securely. Suspend the arm from the neck of the patient, and let it rest for several days.
Then, it is to be loosened. If the joint has been fixed in its place, then unbind it from there and let it rest.
If you see that the joint is not bound well, then repeat the plaster, bind it again, and let it rest for several days until it has healed. Then, it is to be loosened again.
If there is pain after the reduction of the joint and its condition is moving: then administer a moistening in bathing, fine friction, and compression until it settles down, or put over the joint, that is, the fat of the lamb's wool. Then, bind it, and let it rest for a day and night. Then, remove it [the plaster], and immerse it [the affected part] in the bath. When it sweats, then gently friction the joint with a tempered friction. Then, repeat over it three times with the white of an egg, on its first, second, and third entry into the bath, until it becomes softened.
And if you wish, place over it dung of a cow, moistened and heated with butter, and bind it over it.
Do this often. It becomes softened, and returns to its normal condition.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocations by Albucasis.
Albucasis frequently treats a dislocation, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
The reduction of this dislocation is easy. In opposition to the remaining joints. It is necessary that speed be in the reduction of the dislocation at the hour when it occurs, before the location is cut open; or an abscess may occur in it, which is hot.
And the reduction of a dislocation in this is that you place Mahassem, the weak hand, upon a table, and extend the assistant's hand; and the physician places his palm upon the prominent joint, and pushes it until it returns.
Nevertheless, it should be considered if the palm has already retreated to the belly of the hand; then let the weak part of the hand be placed upon a table during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let its position be intrinsically upon the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed upon the table, so that the physician falls upon the prominent joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then tie it with a plaster letting an abscess form; leave it alone, do not touch it again.
For if it cannot be borne, nor can you make the reduction of it after the days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its twist; and nothing harms the weak except that it is softened by the hand, and nothing can be tied; then you will know that the nerves have been severed or rubbed.
It is not clever in this, except to tie it with a cautery near its loss. And perhaps that does not benefit it at all.
When Almahassem does not return, then place a plaster upon him as I have said. Then tie it, and let it be for five days; afterwards it is loosened and let go, and the hand is exercised.
If it is deprived of movement, and something heavy happens to it; then soften it with warm water and rub it occasionally until it becomes soft.
On the treatment of dislocations of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction or towards the back of the palm or the palm's inside, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then hold the head of the finger and turn it towards the direction of the dislocation and let it be for two days.
Then suspend it (the finger) and extend it until it becomes straight on the same day.
When it is night, bind it as described (in the text) and do not cease to loosen it during the day; exercise it with movement, and bind it at night.
Do this for several days until it becomes tight (healed).
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it to that part and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And do the same if it is dislocated in other parts.
On the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When anyone suffers from a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck, or many vertebrae are displaced from their places, there is no cure for it, because death approaches the weak.
And the sign of this is that the sickness departs from him without his will, nor can he hold it; sometimes some of his limbs become soft, or two legs or two arms of his own, or one of them; or a vertebra is displaced from its place.
Often it happens that it (the dislocation) moves and is often removed to the four directions.
That which is moved towards the back is called gibbosity.
The cure for this (disease) is that you consider it if it occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment in it, and there is no healing at all from it.
But in that which occurs due to an accident, or blow, or similar cases, the ancient ones have already explained the modes of treatment in a long discourse. It yields least return when it is profitable (or: when it prospers).
I have already abridged from that which excuses itself little by much due to my proximity to its intention; and in my exposition of it, and the construction of its instrument, I formed it differently than they showed it and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in a hunchback which occurs from the front part, there is no talent, and healing is not from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides as well.
And it is not cured from them (the humpbacks) except that which occurs on the back properly, as I am about to say; and it must be stretched out, the weak one over his face on a platform equal nearly to the wall; and it must be spread beneath him wet linen so that his chest is not injured.
Then a straight piece of wood is placed fixed in a basin on the ground at the end of the platform, around his head; and another piece of wood about his feet at the end of the other platform; and let someone hold the piece of wood, and it shall not be fastened to the basin. And the second person holds the other.
Then bend over the head of the weak one, and under his chin tie a soft but firm bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the piece of wood which is by his head; and tie him there.
Then tighten another bandage over his ankles, and above his knees, and at the fetters (or: shackles) of his feet. Then gather all the ligaments and tie them on another piece of wood that is near his feet.
Then let every servant extend the ligament to the piece of wood, and neither of the woods should be removed from their places where they are fixed; however, they should not be tightly bound, as we said.
The physician shall place his hands on the pelvis with force until he returns; or he may place it on a table. Then he connects his feet to the table until he returns.
If he does not return by this treatment, then take a table whose length is about three arms (elbows). And carve a hole in the wall which we have said is near the sick one, for the end of the table to enter. Then place the middle part of the table on the pelvis. The physician shall then place his feet on the other end (of the table). And the physician binds it tightly until the pelvis is dislocated and returns to its place.
Then, if you wish, work with the lathe which revolves with your hand; and there is, so that you may mark on the ground at the head of the sick one two pieces of wood, whose length is an arm each; and at his head, on the same side, two pieces of wood.
And the length between every piece of wood is a palm; and now a hole has been made in every place where the lathe runs; and each piece of wood must be firmly fixed to the ground so that it does not move at all.
A round piece of wood is inserted; and the lathe, in which the ligament revolves, has two holes on the two pieces of wood.
